Similarly, claiming they were ignorant of rhetoric because they lacked later terminology is equally absurd. The author then questions the rationality of such statements. He states that the opponent's argument would only be valid if it could be proven that the Sahaba died knowing Allah only through taqlid and turned away from the reflection (nazar) that Allah commanded in the Qur'an, and that they did not understand the evidences in the Qur'an—something no believer can accept. The author then praises the intelligence and knowledge of the Sahaba, asserting that even the greatest scholars of later times have not attained the level of religious knowledge that the least of the Sahaba or even their servants and discerning children possessed. This extends also to the Followers and the next generation. Next, the author focuses on 'Ali ibn Abi Talib as a prime example. He relates that 'Ali encountered the innovators and silenced them. He quotes 'Ali saying that if the Prophet had allowed him, he could have placed the entire knowledge of the Qur'an (symbolized by the Fatiha) on seventy camels. The author notes that 'Ali's knowledge was so vast that some innovators were seduced by him and made exaggerated claims about him, similar to what Christians claim about Jesus. Finally, the author gives a specific example of 'Ali's instinctive knowledge: the 'minbar inheritance case' (al-farida al-minbariyya) where a wife, two daughters, and two parents were heirs. Normally, the wife receives 1/8, but the shares add up to more than one. 'Ali solved it on the spot by turning the wife's share from 1/8 to 1/9 (making total 1/9 + 2/3 + 1/3 + 1/3 = ? actually 1/9 + 2/3 + 1/3 + 1/3 = 1/9 + 2/3 + 2/3 = 1/9 + 4/3 = 1/9 + 12/9 = 13/9 >1, but the traditional solution is to reduce the shares proportionally, and the famous answer 'صار ثمنها تسعا' means her eighth became a ninth. The author presents this as evidence of 'Ali's extraordinary intellect. He concludes by challenging the reader to compare the minds of most people to 'Ali's.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, who possesses more religious knowledge: the leading scholars of later times or the least female servant of the Sahaba?
A) The leading scholars of later times
B) The least female servant of the Sahaba
C) They are equal
D) It cannot be compared

Key Takeaway
The Sahaba and early generations possessed a depth of religious knowledge that later scholars cannot reach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly honour the Sahaba as this passage demands, or do you sometimes belittle their knowledge in your heart by thinking modern scholarship has advanced beyond them? How will you guard against that arrogance starting today?
?
If the lowest servant among the Sahaba had more religious knowledge than the greatest scholars of our time, what does that say about your reliance on their transmitted teachings? Are you truly submitting to their authority, or do you pick and choose based on modern preferences?
?
Sanusi says 'how much more necessary is great politeness' for those who speak ill of the Sahaba. Have you ever spoken or thought disrespectfully about a Companion? What concrete step will you take to cultivate reverence for them in your daily life?

The Extraordinary Knowledge of Ali
Sanusi demonstrates Ali's vast knowledge through examples and praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the exceptional intellectual and spiritual knowledge of Ali ibn Abi Talib as presented by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Ali's knowledge was instinctive and vast, solving complex problems instantly.

Sanusi highlights Ali ibn Abi Talib as the epitome of Sahaba knowledge. He cites specific examples: Ali's encounter with innovators, his statement about the Fatiha, the famous hadith 'I am the city of knowledge', and the miraculous solving of the minbar inheritance case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, paying attention to the examples given.
The passage contains a quote from Ali and a hadith.
2
Examine the minbar case
The 'minbar inheritance case' is a specific example where Ali solved a complex inheritance problem instantly. Understand the scenario and his answer.
The problem involved shares adding up to more than the whole; Ali said 'Her eighth has become a ninth.'
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does Sanusi choose these examples? What do they tell us about Ali's knowledge?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَمَّا الأَوَّلُ ـ وهو قوله : مات أبو بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما ولم يعرفا الجوهر والعَرَضَ ، وكذلك سائر الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم - : فأنا أعجبُ أنْ يَذكُرَ مثلَ هذا دليلاً على التقليدِ مَنْ لَهُ أدنى تمييز ، فأَيُّ للألفاظ المصطلح عليها في شيءٍ مِنْ أَدلَّةِ العقائدِ حتى يلزمَ مِنَ الجهل بشيء منها الجهلُ بشيءٍ مِنَ الأدلَّةِ ؟! مدخل وما أشبه هذا بقولِ مَنْ يقولُ : إِنَّ الصحابة رضوانُ اللهِ عليهم كانَتْ تجهل المقصودَ مِنْ علم العربيةِ ؛ لأنَّهم ماتوا ولم يعرفوا حقيقةً الفاعل ولا المفعول ولا الحالِ والتمييز المصطلح عليها عند علماء العربية ، أو كانوا يجهلونَ المقصودَ مِنَ البلاغة ؛ لأنَّهم كانوا يجهلونَ ألفاظاً فيها أحدثها مَنْ بعدهم اصطلاحاً ! وهل هذه الأقوال تصدرُ مِنْ عاقل أُلهِم رشدَهُ ؟! وإِنَّما يصح لهُ الاستدلال لو ثبتَ لهُ أنَّ الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم ماتوا ولم يعرفوا اللهَ إلا بمُجرَّدِ التقليدِ ، وأعرضوا عن النظرِ الذي حصّ تعالى عليه في آي مِنْ كتابِهِ ، وأنَّ أدلَّةَ العقائدِ التي لا تُحصى كثرةً في القرآنِ كانَتْ تمرُّ عليهم ولم يفهموا وجه دلالتِها ، وصحة هذا عنهم مما يأباه كلُّ مؤمن . [ حدَّةُ ذكاء الصحابة الكرامِ رضيَ الله عنهم ووفور علمهم ] وما أحوج مَنْ يُعرِّضُ بمثل هذهِ النقيصة في علي مناصبهم التي لا تُلحَقُ .. لعظيم الأدب ! ولقد نقطعُ أنَّ أكابر علماء زماننا لم يحصل لهم مِنَ العلم بالدين وسننه ما حصل لأدنى أَمَةٍ مِنْ إماء الصحابة رضي الله عنهم أو صبي مُميَّز مِنْ صبيانهم ، وكذا التابعون وتابعوهم بإحسان . ولقد أدرك علي رضي الله عنه زمنَ المبتدعة ، وأفحمهم بما لم يقدروا أن يجدوا معه جواباً ، وحُكِيَ عنه رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ أَنَّهُ قالَ : ( لو أذنَ لي رسولُ اللهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ أنْ أضعَ على « الفاتحةِ » وِقْرَ سبعين بعيراً.. لفعلتُ ) (۱) ، وقالَ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : ( أَنَا مَدِينَةٌ الْعِلْم وَعَلِيٌّ بَابُهَا ) (۲) ، وقد نُقِلَ عنه رضي الله عنه في كل علم العجب العُجاب ، حتى افتتنت بهِ طوائفُ مِنَ المبتدعة وادَّعى بعضُهم فيهِ ما ادَّعَتْهُ النصارى في عيسى عليه السلام . ( ومن عجيبِ أمرِه رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ : أَنَّ مُعضلاتِ المسائل التي لا يُتوصل إلى جوابها إلا بالأنظارِ الدقيقة في السنين المتطاولة .. إذا سُئِلَ هو عنها أجابَ عنها بديهةً مِنْ غيرِ تأمل ولا تعظيم لشأنها ، كأنَّها عنده سؤال الأمور الضرورية ؛ ككون الاثنين مثلاً أكثرَ مِنَ عن الواحد ، وقضاياه في ذلك مشهورةٌ مسطورةٌ في الكُتُب ، وتأمل جوابه رضي الله عنه على المنبر في الفريضة المنبرية ؛ وهي : زوجة وابنتان وأبوان ، وقوله على البديهة بلا تأمل ولا تأخُرٍ في ذلك الموقفِ الصعب : ( صارَ ثُمنُها تُشعاً ) (۳) ، ثم اعرض على عقول أكثر الناسِ ذلك وانظر أين هم ممَّا هنالك
As for the first argument—namely his statement: 'Abu Bakr and 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) died not knowing jawhar (substance) and 'arad (accident), and likewise the rest of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them)'—I am amazed that someone with the least discernment would mention such a thing as evidence for taqlid (blind following). For what connection do the conventionally coined terms have with the proofs of beliefs, so that ignorance of some of them would entail ignorance of some of the proofs?! This resembles the statement of one who says: 'The Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) were ignorant of the purpose of the science of Arabic, because they died without knowing the true nature of the subject (al-fa'il), the object (al-maf'ul), the state (al-hal), and the distinction (al-tamyiz) as conventionally defined by grammarians,' or 'they were ignorant of the purpose of balagha (rhetoric) because they were ignorant of terms coined by later generations as conventions.' Could such statements come from an intelligent person inspired with guidance?! His argument would only be valid if it were established that the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) died knowing Allah only through mere taqlid, and turned away from the reflection (al-nazar) that the Exalted urged in verses of His Book, and that the countless proofs of beliefs in the Qur'an passed them by without their understanding their evidential import. The truth of this about them is something every believer rejects. [The sharpness of intelligence of the noble Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, and the abundance of their knowledge] How much more necessary is great politeness for one who exposes himself to such a deficiency in the lofty stations of those whose stations cannot be attained! We are certain that the leading scholars of our time have not attained from knowledge of the religion and its ways what the lowest female servant of the female servants of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, or a discerning child among their children attained, likewise the Followers and those who followed them in goodness. And indeed 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) encountered the era of the innovators (al-mubtadi'a) and silenced them such that they found no answer. It is narrated from him (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'If the Messenger of Allah (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) had permitted me to place on the Fatiha the load of seventy camels, I would have done so.' And he (may Allah bless him and grant him peace) said: 'I am the city of knowledge and 'Ali is its gate.' It has been reported from him (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding every science that which is wondrously amazing, to the extent that groups of innovators were seduced by him and some claimed about him what the Christians claimed about Jesus (peace be upon him). And among the amazing aspects of his matter (may Allah be pleased with him) is that difficult problems that cannot be solved except by precise reflection over many years—when asked about them, he would answer instinctively without reflection or magnifying their importance, as if they were for him like questions about necessary matters, such as that two is greater than one, for example. His judgments in that regard are famous and recorded in books. Consider his answer (may Allah be pleased with him) on the pulpit regarding the minbar inheritance case: a wife, two daughters, and two parents. He said instinctively without reflection or delay in that difficult situation: 'Her eighth has become a ninth.' Then present that to the minds of most people and see where they are relative to that.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument used by proponents of taqlid (blind following) who claimed that the Sahaba (Companions) did not use kalam terminology like jawhar (substance) and 'arad (accident), and therefore such terminology is unnecessary for faith. The author first expresses astonishment that anyone with discernment would cite this as evidence for taqlid. He argues that the technical terms of kalam have no necessary connection to the actual proofs of beliefs in the Qur'an; ignorance of terms does not imply ignorance of proofs. To illustrate, he provides an analogy: if someone argued that the Sahaba were ignorant of the purpose of Arabic grammar because they did not use the technical terms of grammarians like fa'il, maf'ul, hal, or tamyiz, that would be absurd. Similarly, claiming they were ignorant of rhetoric because they lacked later terminology is equally absurd. The author then questions the rationality of such statements. He states that the opponent's argument would only be valid if it could be proven that the Sahaba died knowing Allah only through taqlid and turned away from the reflection (nazar) that Allah commanded in the Qur'an, and that they did not understand the evidences in the Qur'an—something no believer can accept. The author then praises the intelligence and knowledge of the Sahaba, asserting that even the greatest scholars of later times have not attained the level of religious knowledge that the least of the Sahaba or even their servants and discerning children possessed. This extends also to the Followers and the next generation. Next, the author focuses on 'Ali ibn Abi Talib as a prime example. He relates that 'Ali encountered the innovators and silenced them. He quotes 'Ali saying that if the Prophet had allowed him, he could have placed the entire knowledge of the Qur'an (symbolized by the Fatiha) on seventy camels. He also cites the famous hadith 'I am the city of knowledge and 'Ali is its gate.' The author notes that 'Ali's knowledge was so vast that some innovators were seduced by him and made exaggerated claims about him, similar to what Christians claim about Jesus. Finally, the author gives a specific example of 'Ali's instinctive knowledge: the 'minbar inheritance case' (al-farida al-minbariyya) where a wife, two daughters, and two parents were heirs. Normally, the wife receives 1/8, but the shares add up to more than one. 'Ali solved it on the spot by turning the wife's share from 1/8 to 1/9 (making total 1/9 + 2/3 + 1/3 + 1/3 = ? actually 1/9 + 2/3 + 1/3 + 1/3 = 1/9 + 2/3 + 2/3 = 1/9 + 4/3 = 1/9 + 12/9 = 13/9 >1, but the traditional solution is to reduce the shares proportionally, and the famous answer 'صار ثمنها تسعا' means her eighth became a ninth. The author presents this as evidence of 'Ali's extraordinary intellect. He concludes by challenging the reader to compare the minds of most people to 'Ali's.

Refuting al-Razi on the Sahaba: Knowledge Beyond Terminology
Sanusi refutes al-Razi's statement about the Companions being muqallid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of al-Razi's claim that most Companions were blind followers (muqallid) and his clarification that true knowledge is not tied to later technical terminology.

Core Idea
Certainty of heart, not technical jargon, constitutes true knowledge.

Sanusi strongly defends the Companions against al-Razi's assertion that most were mere followers without proof. He argues that al-Razi mistakenly equates knowledge with the elaborate logical terms developed after the early generations. This lesson unpacks Sanusi's critique and his emphasis on certainty as the real criterion for knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice Sanusi's strong language: 'So behold this statement—how ugly it is!'
Pay attention to the quotation of al-Razi's words.
2
Identify al-Razi's Claim
Al-Razi claims that most Sahaba were muqallid (blind followers) and that this is acceptable, otherwise they would be disbelievers. Sanusi finds this discourteous.
3
Understand Sanusi's Counter
Sanusi diagnoses the root error: al-Razi thought that creedal knowledge requires later terminologies and logical structures. Sanusi counters that knowledge is whatever yields certainty, regardless of wording.
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أساءَ الفخرُ الأدب في حقهم ـ وهي اختلسها الشيطانُ منه - فقالَ : ( الصحيحُ عندنا : أنَّ المقلَّدَ مِنْ أهل النجاة ، وإلا يلزمنا تكفير أكثر الصحابة والتابعينَ ؛ إذ يُعلَمُ بالضرورةِ أنَّ أكثرهم لم يكن عالماً بهذه الأدلَّةِ ) فانظر هذه المقالة ما أشنعها ! ولهُ زلات معروفةٌ في العقائدِ نَبَّهَ عليها ابنُ التِّلِمْساني وغيرُه ، وكأنَّ مقالتَهُ هَذِهِ مقالةُ مَنْ تَوَهَّمَ أَنَّ العقائدَ إِنَّما تُعرَفُ بالتمشدق باصطلاحات أحدثها المتأخرونَ ، وصور تركيبات للأدلة على نهج أصول المنطق لم يعتن بها المتقدمونَ ؛ لأنَّ المقصود إنَّما هو معرفة الحقِّ بما يستلزمه قطعاً ، فكيفما حصل بلفظ
And al-Fakhr (al-Razi) was discourteous toward them—and Satan stole it from him—and he said: 'The correct view with us is that the muqallid (blind follower) is among the people of salvation, otherwise it would necessitate declaring most of the Companions and Successors disbelievers, since it is known by necessity that most of them were not knowledgeable of these proofs.' So behold this statement—how ugly it is! And he has well-known slips in creed which Ibn al-Tilmisani and others have pointed out, and it is as if this statement of his is the statement of one who imagined that creeds are only known by verbose use of terminologies invented by later scholars, and forms of compositions of proofs on the method of the principles of logic with which the early scholars were not concerned, because the goal is only knowledge of the truth by what necessarily entails it, however it is obtained, in whatever wording.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific claim by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi regarding the Companions. Sanusi begins by declaring that al-Razi was discourteous toward the Companions—a discourtesy that Satan stole from him. He then quotes al-Razi's statement: 'The correct view with us is that the muqallid (one who follows without evidence) is among the people of salvation, otherwise it would necessitate declaring most of the Companions and Successors disbelievers, since it is known by necessity that most of them were not knowledgeable of these proofs.' Sanusi exclaims in disapproval, calling this statement extremely ugly. He notes that al-Razi has other well-known slips in creed, pointed out by scholars like Ibn al-Tilmisani. Sanusi then diagnoses the root error: al-Razi imagined that true creedal knowledge only comes from verbose usage of later technical terminology and logical formulations that the early scholars (including the Companions) did not employ. Sanusi counters that the actual goal is knowledge of the truth by whatever necessarily entails it conclusively, regardless of the wording or method used. Thus, the Companions, who had direct certainty and knowledge, are not to be discredited for not using later logical terms.

Key Terms
فخر الدين الرازي
تقليد
أدلة
اصطلاحات
منطق
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only technical logical proofs count as knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that any method yielding certainty is valid; the Companions had certainty without later terminology.

Checkpoint
Why did Sanusi criticize al-Razi's statement about the Sahaba?
A) Because al-Razi praised the Sahaba too much.
B) Because al-Razi claimed that most Sahaba were blind followers who lacked true knowledge.
C) Because al-Razi said the Sahaba were all mujtahids.
D) Because al-Razi denied the prophethood of Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's error was equating knowledge with later logical terms, whereas true knowledge is about certainty however achieved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Do you rely more on your heart's certainty in Allah or on the memorization of proofs? Which does Sanusi say is more fundamental?
?
If you were to defend your faith to a scholar like al-Razi, would you be able to articulate your certainty without fancy terms? What would that look like?

Defending the Sahaba and Refuting al-Razi's Claim
Sanusi cites the hadith and refutes al-Razi's claim about the Sahaba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the high status of the Companions, their role as sources of guidance, and why al-Razi's claim that they were muqallids is rejected.

Core Idea
The Prophet guaranteed that following any Companion leads to guidance; to call them muqallids is a slander.

Sanusi begins by citing the famous hadith equating the Companions to stars, affirming their authority in all religious matters. He then turns sharply to criticize Fakhr al-Din al-Razi for suggesting that most Companions and Followers were muqallids. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion about the nature of true knowledge of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the hadith
Find the Prophet's saying about the Companions like stars. What does it imply about their authority?
The hadith is a key proof of their high status.
2
Note al-Razi's claim and Sanusi's reaction
Al-Razi says the muqallid is among the saved, otherwise he would have to declare most Sahaba and Tabi'un disbelievers. Sanusi calls this ugly and attributes it to Satan.
Notice the strong language.
3
Reflect on what it means for a Companion to have knowledge
Sanusi argues that the Companions were not muqallids because they had decisive knowledge, even if not in formal terms.
This prepares for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقهم ، الصادرة مِنْ ملك الملوك العالم بخفيَّاتِ الضَّمائر ويكفي في إمامتهم لجميع الخلْقِ - ولا يكون كذلكَ إِلا مَنْ بلغَ الرتبة العليا في الاجتهاد - قوله صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : « أَصْحَابِي كَالنُّجُومِ ؛ بِأَيْهِمْ أَقْتَدَيْتُمُ اهْتَدَيْتُمْ (۱) ، وقد كانوا رضي الله عنهم متعرضين لدعاء الخلْقِ إلى الله تعالى ، وإقامةِ حُجَّةِ اللهِ تعالى جميع عليهم ، وإليهم المرجعُ في أزمنتهم في المسائلِ المُعضلة وجميع الحوادثِ النازلة ، وقد أساءَ الفخرُ الأدب في حقهم ـ وهي اختلسها الشيطانُ منه - فقالَ : ( الصحيحُ عندنا : أنَّ المقلَّدَ مِنْ أهل النجاة ، وإلا يلزمنا تكفير أكثر الصحابة والتابعينَ ؛ إذ يُعلَمُ بالضرورةِ أنَّ أكثرهم لم يكن عالماً بهذه الأدلَّةِ ((۲) و جُلْسَةٌ فانظر هذه المقالة ما أشنعها ! ولهُ زلات معروفةٌ في العقائدِ نَبَّهَ عليها ابنُ التِّلِمْساني وغيرُه (۳) ، وكأنَّ مقالتَهُ هَذِهِ مقالةُ مَنْ تَوهَّمَ أَنَّ العقائدَ إِنَّما تُعرَفُ بالتمشدق باصطلاحات أحدثها المتأخرونَ ، وصور تركيبات للأدلة على نهج أصول المنطق لم يعتنِ بها المتقدمونَ ؛ لأنَّ المقصود إنَّما هو معرفة الحقِّ بما يستلزمه قطعاً ، فكيفما حصل بلفظ أو بغيرِ لفظ ، بتركيب مخصوص أو غيره .. حصل المقصود ، ولا حاجة إلى زيادة ، والنفوس الزكيَّةُ القدسيَّةُ غنية في أنظارها عن تلك القوانين المصطلح عليها كلها ، بل عقلُ مَنِ استنبطَها بالنسبة إلى تلك النفوس كنقطةٍ مِنْ بحارِ الدنيا كلها وقد سمعت بعض أجوبة عليّ رضي الله عنه على البديهة فيما سبق (۱) ، وإنّما أحدثَ المتأخّرونَ مِنَ الاصطلاحاتِ ما أحدثوا ؛ لتَخِفَّ المؤنة عليهم في التعلم والتعليم ، لا لأن معرفة الحق موقوفةٌ عليها وإلى هذا المعنى أشار أبو بكر بنُ فُورَكَ بقولِهِ : ( لو لم يدخل الجنةَ إِلا مَنْ عرفَ الجوهر والعرض .. لبقيَتْ خاليةً ) ، ونحنُ نقولُ بموجبه ، ونقولُ معَ ذلكَ : لا يدخلُ الجنةَ إِلا مَنْ هو عارف بالله تعالى ولم يُقلّد في ذلك ، عرف الجوهر والعرض أو لم يعرفهما ، فليس في قولِ ابنِ فُورَكَ ما يدلُّ على صحةِ التقليدِ ، ولا في عدم اطلاع الصحابة على اصطلاحات أحدثَها المتأخّرونَ ما يدلُّ على أَنَّهم كانوا مقلدينَ ومَنْ ظَنَّ بالصحابة رضي الله عنهم أنَّهم كانوا في إيمانِهم مقلدين فقد أعظم عليهمُ الفِرية (۲) ، وجهل قدرَهمُ الأعظم . وقد كانَ سائرُ الكَفَرَةِ مِنَ الأَعاجِمِ يَذْبُونَ عن دينهم ودينِ آبائهم
Their right, emanating from the King of kings, the Knower of the secrets of consciences, and sufficient for their leadership over all creation - and no one is like that except one who has reached the highest rank in ijtihad - is his saying, may God bless him and grant him peace: 'My companions are like stars; whichever of them you follow, you will be guided (1).' And they, may God be pleased with them, were engaged in calling creation to God Almighty, and establishing the proof of God Almighty against them, and to them was the reference in their times in difficult matters and all occurring incidents. And al-Fakhr [al-Razi] behaved disrespectfully towards them - and Satan snatched it from him - so he said: 'The correct view with us is that the muqallid is among the people of salvation, otherwise we would be forced to declare the majority of the Companions and Followers disbelievers, since it is known by necessity that most of them were not knowledgeable of these proofs (2).' And a session? So look at this statement, how ugly it is! He has known slips in creed that Ibn al-Tilmisani and others pointed out (3). It is as if his statement is the statement of one who imagined that creeds are only known by babbling with terminologies invented by later scholars, and forms of combinations of proofs on the model of the principles of logic that the early scholars did not heed. Because the intention is only knowledge of the truth by what necessitates it decisively. However it is obtained, with wording or without wording, with a specific combination or otherwise... the intention is achieved, and there is no need for more. And pure, holy souls are independent in their reflections from all of those conventional laws. Rather, the intellect of the one who derived them compared to those souls is like a drop from all the seas of the world. And I have heard some of Ali's, may God be pleased with him, instantaneous answers to what came before (1). Indeed, the later scholars invented the terminologies they invented only to lighten the burden for them in learning and teaching, not because knowledge of the truth depends on them. And Abu Bakr ibn Furak indicated this meaning by his saying: 'If none would enter Paradise except one who knows substance and accident... it would remain empty.' And we say in accordance with it, and we say along with that: None enters Paradise except one who is knowledgeable of God Almighty and did not follow taqlid in that, whether he knows substance and accident or does not know them. So there is nothing in Ibn Furak's saying that indicates the validity of taqlid, nor in the Companions' lack of knowledge of terminologies invented by later scholars is there any indication that they were muqallids. And whoever thinks that the Companions, may God be pleased with them, were muqallids in their faith has indeed magnified the slander against them (2) and ignored their great status. And all the disbelievers among the non-Arabs used to defend their religion and the religion of their fathers.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Companions (Sahaba) and a refutation of al-Razi's claim that most of them were muqallids (followers of authority without proof). The author begins by stating the high status of the Sahaba, citing the Prophet's hadith that they are like stars and that following any of them leads to guidance. He then criticizes al-Razi for his disrespectful statement that the muqallid is among the saved, otherwise one would have to declare most Sahaba and Followers disbelievers. The author finds this shocking and attributes it to Satan. He explains that al-Razi's misunderstanding stems from imagining that creedal knowledge is only obtained through the technical terminologies and argumentative structures invented by later scholars. The true purpose is to know the truth by whatever necessitates it decisively, regardless of wording or form. Pure souls do not need such contrived laws. The author then references a quick answer of Ali (as a model of intuitive knowledge) and states that later scholars only developed terminologies to facilitate teaching and learning, not because knowledge of God depends on them. He quotes Ibn Furak's statement that if only those who know substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad) enter Paradise, it would remain empty. The author affirms this but adds that entering Paradise requires knowledge of God without taqlid, whether or not one knows those technical terms. Therefore, lack of such knowledge in the Sahaba does not prove they were muqallids. Finally, he warns against accusing the Sahaba of being muqallids, likening it to a slander, and notes that even disbelievers defend their inherited religion zealously.

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid)
صحابة (Sahaba)
اجتهاد (ijtihad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Companions were muqallids because they did not use later theological terminologies.
Correction: Sanusi shows that they had decisive knowledge through their own proofs, even if not in formal terms, as evidenced by the hadith.

Checkpoint
What did al-Razi claim about the Sahaba?
A) They were all mujtahids.
B) Most of them were muqallids.
C) They knew all the formal proofs.
D) They were disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The Companions possessed decisive knowledge of God without relying on later technical terms, and to call them muqallids is a grave error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone dismiss the Companions for lacking later theological terms, does your heart defend them or do you feel a twinge of agreement? What does that reveal about your own attachment to their legacy?
?
If you were to follow a scholar who downplays the Companions' knowledge, would you be able to recognize you're straying from the path of the Salaf? What might you sacrifice by prioritizing intellectual comfort over truth?
?
Search your heart: Is there any hidden pride in your own learning that makes you look down on those early Muslims as naive? How would you respond if you were to meet a Companion tomorrow and knew you had entertained such thoughts?

True Knowledge of Allah and the Role of Terminology
Sanusi explains true knowledge of Allah and refutes the necessity of technical terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge of Allah does not depend on later theological terminologies, but on decisive proof, and that the Companions possessed such knowledge without those terms.

Core Idea
Knowledge of Allah is achieved through decisive proof, not through jargon.

Sanusi continues by analyzing al-Razi's error more deeply: he mistakenly thought that creedal knowledge depends on the technical apparatus of later theologians. Sanusi explains that the early Muslims attained certainty without such formulations, and that later terminologies are merely teaching aids. He supports this with a quote from Ibn Furak and an example from Ali's quick answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify Sanusi's critique
Sanusi says al-Razi imagines that creeds are only known by babbling with terminologies. What is the alternative he presents?
2
Understand the principle of decisive knowledge
The intention is knowledge of truth by what necessitates it decisively, whether with wording or without, with a specific combination or not.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Note the examples: Ali and Ibn Furak
Ali's quick answer shows intuitive knowledge. Ibn Furak said if only those who know substance and accident enter Paradise, it would be empty.
These illustrate that technical terms are not required.
4
Conclude about the Companions
Lack of technical terms does not imply taqlid. The Companions had knowledge of Allah without them.
This refutes al-Razi.

Why Later Muslims Face Subtle Doubts
Sanusi contrasts the prophetic era with later times regarding doubts and innovations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Companions were not explicitly warned about subtle doubts and why later generations must be more vigilant in distinguishing truth from innovation.

Core Idea
The light of prophethood shielded the Companions from doubts we now face.

Sanusi highlights a crucial difference between the prophetic era and later times. The Companions lived in the direct light of prophethood, which eliminated ignorance and devilish whisperings, making truth and falsehood starkly clear. Today, the Sunnah is rare among innovations, requiring scholars to address subtle doubts that early Muslims never encountered. This passage explains why later Islamic scholarship must devote greater effort to refuting doubts and clarifying the truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's contrast between the two eras sink in.
Notice the vivid imagery: lights of prophethood, darknesses of ignorance, fires of devils.
2
Identify the two eras described
Find where Sanusi describes the prophetic era (the Companions' time) and where he shifts to later times (our times). Notice how the conditions differ.
The pivotal phrase is 'غاية الأمر' which signals the main point.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does this difference matter for Islamic scholarship? What does it say about the necessity of scholarly work in later ages?
This connects to the learning objective about increased vigilance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غاية الأمر : أَنَّ القومَ الذين شاهدوه رضيَ اللهُ عنهم لَمَّا أَنْ أَشْرَقَتْ عليهم أنوار النبوَّةِ ، وتلاشَتْ معها ظلمات الجهل والوسواس ، وخَمَدَتْ عندَها نيران شياطين الإنس والجن .. لم ينبهوا صريحاً على دقائقِ الشُّبَهِ وخفيَّاتِ الأمراض التي ابتُلِيَ بها مَنْ بعدَهم ؛ لأنَّها لم تطرق منيع ساحتِهم ، ولا حَلَّتْ برفيع جُوارِهم ، ولا لاحَ قَزَعُها في صفاء شمسهم وارتفاع نهارهم وإنَّما الناسُ في ذلك الزمانِ أحد رجلين : مؤمن ولي ، أو كافر شقي ، وأما أزمنتنا هذهِ فالسنة فيها بين البدع كالشعرة البيضاء في رواه أبو نعيم في ( الحلية » ( ٤ / ٢٦ ) .
The main point: that the people who saw him, may Allah be pleased with them, when the lights of prophethood shone upon them, and the darknesses of ignorance and whisperings vanished with them, and the fires of the devils of men and jinn were extinguished at them... they were not explicitly warned about the subtleties of doubts and the hidden diseases with which those after them were afflicted, because they did not approach their invincible arena, nor did they settle in their exalted neighborhood, nor did their clouds appear in the purity of their sun and the height of their day. And the people in that time were only two types of men: a believing saint or a disbelieving wretch. As for our times, the Sunnah among innovations is like a white hair... Narrated by Abu Nu'aym in al-Hilyah (4/26).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory contrast. The author summarizes the situation: those who witnessed the Prophet were bathed in the light of prophethood, which eliminated ignorance and devilish influences. Therefore, they did not need explicit warnings against subtle doubts and hidden spiritual diseases—issues that later generations face because these problems did not affect them. People in that era were clearly divided into either believing saints or disbelieving wretches, with no ambiguous middle ground. In contrast, the author notes that in later times, the Sunnah has become rare among innovations, comparing it to a white hair (on a black bull, implied). This explains why later scholars must devote effort to refute doubts and clarify the truth.

Key Terms
غاية الأمر
الشبه
السنة
البدع
أنوار النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Companions were infallible or that later believers cannot attain similar certainty.
Correction: The passage stresses the unique context of prophethood, not infallibility. Later believers can achieve certainty through scholarship and vigilance, but the environment is different.

Checkpoint
Why did the Companions not need explicit warnings about subtle doubts and diseases?
A) Because they were all infallible
B) Because the light of prophethood eliminated such issues and they never faced them
C) Because they were not intelligent enough
D) Because they relied on miracles alone

Key Takeaway
The unique context of the prophetic era shielded the Companions from subtle doubts; later Muslims must be more diligent in distinguishing Sunnah from innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doubt about your faith, do you turn immediately to the prophetic guidance in the Quran and Sunnah, or do you rely on your own reasoning or popular opinions? What is one doubt you have entertained that you now see as a hidden disease?
?
This passage says that in the prophetic era people were either believing saints or disbelieving wretches. In your own life, are you living as a clear believer or are you comfortable with a mixture of Sunnah and innovation? What is one innovation you have accepted that you now need to abandon?
?
The Sunnah is described as a white hair among black innovations. Are you actively seeking that white hair, or are you following the crowd? What specific step will you take today to ensure your practice aligns with the prophetic way rather than prevailing customs?

Consensus of All Religions on the Temporal Origination of the World
Sanusi notes the universal agreement of religious communities on the creation of the universe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in the creation of the universe is a universal religious consensus, not limited to Islam.

Core Idea
All religions agree: everything besides God is created.

Sanusi argues that the temporal origination of the world is not a uniquely Islamic doctrine but a principle shared across all major religious traditions. By citing agreement from Jews, Christians, and even Magians, he strengthens the Sunni position that creation is a rational necessity, isolating the philosophical opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the plain meaning.
Notice the emphatic tone: 'all religious communities'.
2
Identify the groups mentioned
List the religious communities Sanusi names: Jews, Christians, Magians. Note that he includes even the Magians (Zoroastrians) to show the consensus is truly universal.
3
Understand the significance of including Magians
The Magians are often considered dualists; their inclusion shows that even those with a different concept of God still affirm creation.
This widens the consensus beyond monotheists.
4
Note the exception: the philosophers
Sanusi states that only a small group of philosophers (falāsifa) dissented, and some self-ascribing Muslims followed them.
This sets up the philosophers as the primary opponents of the temporal origination doctrine.
5
Reflect on the argumentative strategy
Consider how using consensus (ijmā‘) of all religions strengthens the case: it is not just an Islamic claim but a universal religious truth.
This preempts objections that the doctrine is sectarian.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنَّ الملل كلَّها أجمعَتْ على حدوثِ ما سوى اللهِ جلَّ ، حتى اليهود والنصارى وحتى المجوس ، ولم يخالف في ذلكَ إلا شرذمةٌ مِنَ الفلاسفة ، وتبعَهم على ذلكَ بعضُ مَنْ يَنسِبُ نفسَهُ
Know that all religious communities (milal) have agreed upon the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of what is apart from God, exalted, even the Jews, Christians, and even the Magians. None opposed this except a small group among the philosophers (falāsifa), and some self-ascribing [to Islam] followed them in that.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a brief historical and doctrinal note establishing the near-universal consensus on the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of everything other than God. The author lists major religious communities: Jews, Christians, and even the Magians (Zoroastrians). The only dissenters are a small group of philosophers (falāsifa) and some Muslims who follow them. This consensus strengthens the Sunni position by showing that belief in creation is not sect-specific but shared across traditions, isolating the philosophical opposition.

Key Terms
الملل
الحدوث
الفلاسفة
اليهود
النصارى
المجوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief in creation is unique to Islam.
Correction: Sanusi shows that Jews, Christians, and Magians also affirm the temporal origination of the universe, making it a universal religious tenet.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which groups are unanimous in believing that everything besides God is temporally originated?
A) Only Muslims
B) Jews, Christians, Magians, and all religious communities
C) Only philosophers
D) Only Jews and Christians

Key Takeaway
The universal consensus on creation across religions underscores that belief in a temporal universe is a foundational religious truth, supported by reason and scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all religions agree that everything besides God is created, why do you often act as if this world is permanent? Where in your daily life do you cling to things as if they will never end?
?
The Magians held false beliefs about God, yet they still affirmed creation. You claim to know the Creator—does your life reflect that you truly believe everything else will perish? What would change if you lived with that conviction today?
?
The philosophers rejected this consensus due to their own reasoning. Are there areas in your faith where you trust your own intellect over the clear agreement of believers? What would it cost you to submit completely?

Universal Consensus on Temporal Origin and Refutation of Philosophers
Sanusi states the universal consensus on temporal origin and refutes philosopher's views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all religious communities agree on the temporal origin of creation, and you will learn the key arguments of the philosophers that contradict this consensus and why they are rejected.

Core Idea
All religious communities agree on creation's temporality; only a fringe of philosophers dissent.

This lesson begins with a remarkable claim: every religious community—Jews, Christians, Magians—agrees that everything other than God is temporally originated. Only a small group of philosophers opposed this, leading to complex theories about eternal entities. We'll explore these views and see why the author dismisses them as irrational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the strong assertion of consensus and the detailed summary of the philosophers' positions.
Pay attention to the list of five eternal entities.
2
Identify the consensus and the dissenting group
The author states that all religious communities agreed on the temporal origin of everything besides God. Identify which group opposed this consensus.
The dissenting group is a small group of philosophers.
3
Study the philosophers' five eternal entities
The ancient philosophers affirmed five eternal entities. List them and understand what each refers to: the Necessary Existent (called 'Aql), Nafs, Hayula, Dahr, and Khala'.
These are the foundations of their worldview.
4
Examine the later philosophers' modifications
Later philosophers modified the theory, distinguishing between the celestial and sublunar worlds. Note how they claimed motions and forms are eternal in kind but temporally originated in particulars.
The chicken-and-egg example illustrates their view.
5
Reflect on the author's critique
The author dismisses these views as extremely weak and unacceptable. Consider why he uses such strong language and what this teaches about the role of consensus in Islamic theology.
Galen himself hesitated, showing the weakness of their claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم: أنَّ الملل كلَّها أجمعَتْ على حدوثِ ما سوى اللهِ جلَّ، حتى اليهود والنصارى وحتى المجوس، ولم يخالف في ذلكَ إلا شرذمةٌ مِنَ الفلاسفة، وتبعَهم على ذلكَ بعضُ مَنْ يَنسِبُ نفسَهُ للإسلام وليسَ لهُ فيه نصيبٌ، والاشتغال بتفصيل مذاهبهم في ذلكَ يطولُ. والحاصل منهُ: أنَّ قدماءَهم أثبتوا قدماء خمسةً: واجب الوجودِ وسموه عقلاً، ثم نفساً، وهَيُولى، ودهراً، وخلاءً، وصار جماعةٌ مِنْ متأخّريهم: إلى أنَّ العالم العلوي قديم بذاتِهِ وصفاته إلا الحركاتِ؛ فإنَّها حادثة بأشخاصها، قديمة بأنواعها، فلا حركة إلا وقبلها حركة لا إلى أوَّل، وأمَّا العالم السفلي - وهو عالم الكون والفساد، وهو ما تحتَ مُقصَّرِ فلكِ القمر - فقالوا: إِنَّ هَيُولاهُ قديمةٌ، وكلَّ ما فيهِ مِنَ الصور والأعراض حادثة بأشخاصها قديمة بأنواعها، فلا ولدَ إلا وقبلَهُ والدٌ، ولا بيضةَ إِلا مِنْ دجاجة، ولا دجاجة إلا مِنْ بيضة، ولا زرع إلا مِنْ بِزْرِ، وتوقَّفَ جالينوس في قدم ما ادَّعَوا قدمَهُ ومذاهبهم في ذلك ركيكة جداً، لا يرضى بمقالتها مؤمن بل ولا مطلق عاقل، إلا مَنْ سُلِبَ عقله وإيمانه؛ فإنَّهُ لا حول ولا قُوَّةَ إلا بالله.
Know that all religious communities have agreed on the temporal origin of everything other than God, may He be exalted, even the Jews, Christians, and even the Magians. No one opposed that except a small group of philosophers, and they were followed in that by some who claim Islam but have no share in it. A detailed exposition of their sects in this matter would be lengthy. The summary of it is that their ancients affirmed five eternal entities: the Necessary Existent (which they called 'Aql), then Nafs, Hayula, Dahr, and Khala'. Then a group of their later ones came to hold that the celestial world is eternal in its essence and attributes except for motions, for they are temporally originated in their particulars but eternal in their kinds, so there is no motion except that before it is a motion without a first. As for the sublunar world—which is the world of generation and corruption, and it is what is below the sphere of the moon—they said that its hylic matter is eternal, and everything in it of forms and accidents is temporally originated in its particulars but eternal in its kinds. So no child except that before it is a parent, no egg except from a chicken, no chicken except from an egg, no plant except from seed. Galen hesitated regarding the eternity of what they claimed to be eternal. Their sects in this are very weak; no believer would be satisfied with their statement, nor even any rational person, except one whose intellect and faith have been stripped away. There is no power and no strength except through God.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a statement of consensus (ijma') and a refutation of the philosophers. The author first notes the universal agreement of all religious communities (including Jews, Christians, and Magians) on the temporal origin (huduth) of the universe. Only a small group of philosophers opposed this, followed by some who falsely claim Islam. The author then summarizes the philosophers' views: the ancients affirmed five eternal entities: the Necessary Existent (called 'Aql), Nafs, Hayula (matter), Dahr (time), and Khala' (void). Later philosophers held that the celestial world is eternal in essence and attributes, but its motions are temporally originated in their individual instances while eternal in kind (i.e., each motion is preceded by another). The sublunar world (of generation and corruption) has eternal hylic matter, but its forms and accidents are temporally originated in their particulars though eternal in kind, represented by the cycle of chicken and egg. Galen himself hesitated on their claims. The author declares these views extremely weak, unacceptable to any believer or rational person, only to those stripped of intellect and faith.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think only Muslims believe in the temporal origin of the universe, but the author shows it is a universal consensus of all religious communities.
Correction: The text explicitly states that even Jews, Christians, and Magians agreed on the temporal origin of everything besides Allah. Only a small group of philosophers opposed.

Checkpoint

A) The Jews.
B) The Christians.
C) A small group of philosophers.
D) The Magians.

Key Takeaway
The unanimous consensus of all religious communities on the temporal origin of creation is a powerful argument, and the philosophers' alternative views are weak and irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that even Jews and Magians agreed on the temporal origin of creation. If such an admission comes from non-believers, does your own acknowledgment of Allah as the sole Eternal carry the same weight of certainty, or is it merely lip service? Tonight, take ten minutes to meditate on the meaning of 'al-Awwal' (The First) and ask yourself: what would it mean for your life if you truly believed nothing besides Allah is eternal?
?
The philosophers claimed that every motion is preceded by another motion without a first—conveniently avoiding a beginning. Are there habits or sins in your life that you treat as 'eternal cycles'—rationalized as just the way things are, never decisively ended? This week, identify one such pattern and consciously interrupt it with a new action, recognizing that Allah alone is the First, and your patterns have a beginning that can end.
?
Imam Sanusi says no rational person would accept the philosophers' views except one stripped of intellect and faith. Is there any area of your life where you trust your own reasoning over clear revelation? Where have you allowed your intellect to doubt or rationalize away a clear command of Allah? Repent of that pride today and ask Allah to align your mind with His truth.

The Five Eternal Principles of the Ancient Philosophers
Sanusi enumerates the five eternal things of ancient philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five eternal principles that ancient philosophers posited and how they conflict with Islamic monotheism.

Core Idea
Ancient philosophers claimed five eternal things, including Allah, as co-eternal principles.

Sanusi begins his critique by listing the ancient philosophers' belief in five eternal principles. They considered the Necessary Existence (which they called intellect), soul, matter, time, and void as all eternal. This directly contradicts the Islamic teaching that only Allah is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage listing the five eternal things. Notice the terms used for each.
The passage is short and enumerative.
2
Identify the five items
List the five eternal principles: Necessary Existence (called intellect), soul, matter, time, and void.
These are all considered uncreated and eternal.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on how this doctrine denies Allah's sole eternity and pairs Him with created things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل منهُ : أنَّ قدماءَهم أثبتوا قدماء خمسةً : واجب الوجودِ وسموه عقلاً (۱) ، ثم نفساً ، وهَيُولى ، ودهراً، وخلاءً ، وصار جماعةٌ مِنْ متأخّريهم : إلى أنَّ العالم العلوي قديم بذاتِهِ وصفاته إلا الحركاتِ ؛ فإنَّها حادثة بأشخاصها ، قديمة بأنواعها ، فلا حركة إلا وقبلها حركة لا إلى أوَّل ، وأمَّا العالم السفلي - وهو عالم الكون والفساد ، وهو ما تحتَ مُقصَّرِ فلكِ القمر - فقالوا : إِنَّ هَيُولاهُ قديمةٌ . وكلَّ ما فيهِ مِنَ الصور والأعراض حادثة بأشخاصها قديمة بأنواعها ، فلا ولدَ إلا وقبلَهُ والدٌ ، ولا بيضةَ إِلا مِنْ دجاجة ، ولا دجاجة إلا مِنْ بيضة ، ولا زرع إلا مِنْ بِزْرِ ، وتوقَّفَ جالينوس في قدم ما ادَّعَوا قدمَهُ ومذاهبهم في ذلك ركيكة جداً ، لا يرضى بمقالتها مؤمن بل ولا مطلق عاقل ، إلا مَنْ سُلِبَ عقله وإيمانه ؛ فإنَّهُ لا حول ولا قُوَّةَ إلا بالله.
(۱) ليس أحد العقول العشرة عند من أثبتها ؛ لأنها أثرُ واجب الوجود ، ولا يسميه عقلاً . « حامدي » ( ص ١٣٤ ) .
And the summary of it: that their ancients affirmed five eternal things: the Necessary Existence, and they called it intellect (1), then soul, matter, time, and void. And a group of their later ones came to hold that the upper world is eternal in its essence and attributes except the movements; for they are contingent in their individuals, eternal in their kinds, so no movement except before it a movement without a first. And as for the lower world - which is the world of generation and corruption, and it is what is below the convexity of the sphere of the moon - they said: its matter is eternal. And everything in it of forms and accidents is contingent in its individuals, eternal in its kinds: no child except before it a parent, no egg except from a hen, and no hen except from an egg, and no crop except from a seed. And Galen hesitated about the eternity of what they claimed to be eternal. And their doctrines in that are very flimsy; no believer is satisfied with their statement, nor even any rational person at all, except one whose intellect and faith have been stripped; for there is no power nor strength except with God.
(1) It is not one of the ten intellects according to those who affirm them; because it is an effect of the Necessary Existence, and they do not call it intellect. [Hamidi (p. 134)].
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage summarizes the philosophical doctrines on the eternity of the world. The author first describes the ancient philosophers who posited five eternal principles: the Necessary Existence (which they called 'intellect'), soul, matter, time, and void. Then he describes later philosophers who held that the upper world (celestial realm) is eternal in essence and attributes, except that individual movements are contingent (occur in time) but their kind is eternal—there is no first movement. For the lower world (sublunary), they considered matter eternal, while forms and accidents are contingent in their individuals but their kind is eternal—hence endless causal chains without a first cause like parent-child, hen-egg cycles. Galen hesitated about these claims. The author dismisses these doctrines as extremely weak, unacceptable to any believer or rational person except one stripped of intellect and faith. A footnote clarifies that the 'intellect' here is not one of the ten intellects of later philosophy, but an effect of the Necessary Existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That philosophers believed only in one eternal being (Allah).
Correction: Actually, ancient philosophers affirmed five eternal principles, including matter, time, etc., which is polytheistic.

Checkpoint
How many eternal principles did the ancient philosophers affirm?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The ancient philosophers wrongly considered five things eternal, including Allah as 'intellect', thereby violating tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you prioritize your career, family, or comfort over obedience to Allah, are you not, in a sense, treating these as co-eternal principles alongside Him? How would you dismantle that subtle shirk in your heart starting today?
?
The philosophers placed 'time' as eternal. Where in your life do you act as if time is an independent reality that controls you, rather than a creation of Allah? What one change can you make to acknowledge Allah's sole lordship over time?
?
If Allah is the only Necessary Existence, why do you still feel a sense of necessity toward created things like praise, security, or possessions? How can you train your heart to rely on Allah alone as the source of all that is essential?

Later Philosophers on Upper and Lower Worlds
Sanusi explains later philosophers' doctrine on upper and lower worlds and dismisses it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the later philosophers' view that the world is eternal in kind but its individuals are contingent, leading to infinite causal chains, and Sanusi's rejection of this doctrine.

Core Idea
Later philosophers claimed eternal kinds but contingent individuals, leading to infinite regress and denial of a first cause.

Sanusi now presents the more refined doctrine of later philosophers, who distinguished between the celestial and sublunary realms. They held that the upper world is eternal except for individual movements, and the lower world has eternal matter but contingent forms and accidents – resulting in endless cycles like the hen-egg problem. Sanusi dismisses this as flimsy and unacceptable to any rational person.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the passage describing the later philosophers' views on the upper and lower worlds.
The passage includes examples: hen-egg, seed-crop.
2
Distinguish the two realms
Note how the upper world (celestial) is said to be eternal in essence/attributes except movements; the lower world (sublunary) has eternal matter but contingent forms/accidents.
Both realms are claimed to have eternal types but transient individuals.
3
Understand the infinite regress
The examples (no child without parent, no egg without hen) illustrate that they deny a first cause, holding an endless chain.
4
See Sanusi's judgment
Read the final sentence where Sanusi calls the doctrine flimsy and says no believer or rational person accepts it.
He even mentions Galen's hesitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?

Refutation of the Trinity
Sanusi refutes the Christian Trinity by logical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's logical refutation of the Christian doctrine of the three hypostases (Trinity) and why he considers it contradictory.

Core Idea
The Trinity combines unity and multiplicity—a logical impossibility.

Sanusi now turns to refute a major doctrine of Christianity: the Trinity. He first explains what Christians believe—that God is one substance with three hypostases—and then exposes the logical contradiction at its heart. This passage is a clear example of kalam reasoning applied to interfaith debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Sanusi begins by stating the belief and then refutes it.
2
Identify the three hypostases
Find the three hypostases (existence, knowledge, life) and their corresponding names (Father, Son, Holy Spirit). Understand the Christian claim that these three are one god.
3
Understand the logical contradiction
Sanusi argues that claiming three are one combines unity and multiplicity, which are opposites. Reflect on why this is impossible in rational thought.
4
Examine the status of the hypostases
He says the hypostases are mere states or considerations that exist only in the mind, not in reality. Grasp why this makes the doctrine unintelligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلانُ القول بالأقانيم الثلاثةِ ] وإذا عرفت هذا عرفت استحالة التجزئة التي أثبتها النصارى لإلههم ، تعالى الله عزَّ وجلَّ عن ذلكَ علوّاً كبيراً ؛ فإنَّهم اعتقدوا أنَّ معبودهم جوهرُ ؛ أي : أصلُ الأقانيم ؛ وذلكَ أَنَّ لهُ عندَهم ثلاثةَ أقانيم : أُقنوم الوجودِ : ويُعبرونَ عنه بالأب ، وأقنوم العلم : ويُعبِّرُونَ عنه بالابن والكلمةِ ، وأقنوم الحياةِ : ويُعبّرون عنه بروح القدس . ثم قالوا : إنَّ مجموع الثلاثةِ إله واحد ! فجمعوا بين نقيضين : وحدة وكثرة ، وجعلوا الذات تتركَّبُ مِنْ مُجرَّدِ أحوالٍ لا وجود لها و اعتبارات لا تُوجَدُ إلا في الأذهان ، وذلك غير معقول لعاقل . والأقنوم : كلمة يونانية ، والمراد بها في تلك اللغة : أصلُ الشيء ، ويعني بها النصارى : الأصل الذي كانَتْ عنه حقيقة إلههم ، وقد طُولبوا في دليل الحصر في الثلاثةِ ، فقالوا : لأنَّ الخلق والإبداع
Refutation of the doctrine of the three hypostases. And if you know this, you know the impossibility of the division which the Christians have affirmed for their god — exalted is God above that greatly. For they believed that their worshipped is a substance, i.e., the origin of the hypostases. That is because for them He has three hypostases: the hypostasis of existence, which they call the Father; the hypostasis of knowledge, which they call the Son and the Word; and the hypostasis of life, which they call the Holy Spirit. Then they said: the sum of the three is one god! So they combined two opposites: unity and multiplicity, and they made the essence composed of mere states that have no existence and considerations that exist only in minds, and that is not intelligible to a rational person. And 'uqnūm' is a Greek word, meaning in that language: the origin of a thing; and by it the Christians mean the origin from which the reality of their god came. And they were asked for evidence of the limitation to three, so they said: because creation and origination
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Christian doctrine of the Trinity (الأقانيم الثلاثة). The author explains that Christians believe their god is a substance (جوهر) that is the origin of three hypostases (أقانيم): the hypostasis of existence (Father), of knowledge (Son/Word), and of life (Holy Spirit). They claim these three together are one god. The refutation points out that this combines two contradictories: unity and multiplicity. Furthermore, the hypostases are mere states or considerations that have no real existence outside the mind; making the divine essence composed of such mental constructs is unintelligible. The author also notes the Greek origin of the word 'uqnūm' (origin) and that Christians were challenged to prove why there are exactly three hypostases, to which they replied 'because creation and origination...' (the argument is cut off).

Key Terms
أقانيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Trinity is a mysterious truth that cannot be understood rationally.
Correction: Sanusi argues it is a logical contradiction (unity and multiplicity) and therefore impossible, not mysterious.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical contradiction Sanusi identifies in the Trinity?
A) It denies God's power
B) It combines unity and multiplicity
C) It uses Greek terms
D) It limits God to three persons

Key Takeaway
The Christian Trinity is rationally impossible because it requires the combination of unity and multiplicity, and its components are mere mental constructs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read Sanusi's logical refutation of the Trinity. In your own religious life, do you hold any beliefs that combine contradictory ideas? Are you willing to examine them with the same critical standard?
?
Sanusi says the hypostases are 'mere states that have no existence and considerations that exist only in minds.' Is there any concept you worship or rely upon that might be a human mental construct rather than a divine reality? How would you know?
?
The passage ends with a challenge to Christians for evidence of the limitation to three. Do you accept doctrines in your faith without demanding rational evidence? How does this lesson call you to intellectual honesty before God?

Refuting the Incarnation: Argument from Union
Sanusi refutes the incarnation by showing union leads to separation of the Godhead.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Christian doctrine of incarnation by demonstrating that any claimed union between the Word and Jesus leads to the dissolution of the Godhead.

Core Idea
Union implies separation—if the Word joined Jesus, the Godhead is divided.

Sanusi now applies the principle that God cannot be in need of a locus or unite with creation to a specific false doctrine: the Christian claim that the hypostasis of the Word united with Jesus. By analyzing what such a union could mean, he shows that it leads to absurdity—the very idea of union ends up destroying the unity of God. This passage is a sharp logical refutation of incarnation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation to get the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the conditional opening.
2
Identify the key principle
The argument builds on the impossibility of God needing a locus or uniting. Understand why that is a foundation.
This comes from previous lesson, but is restated here.
3
Examine the Christian claim
The Christians claim the Word (hypostasis) united with Jesus' humanity. Note the term 'nasut'.
They differed on the meaning of union.
4
Analyze the logical consequence
If union means the Word subsists in Jesus like an accident, then the Word must leave the divine essence, since one meaning cannot subsist in two essences.
This leads to a divided Godhead.
5
Realize the absurd conclusion
The remaining divine essence is not fully God, and Jesus only has a part of God. Thus, according to their own logic, God does not exist.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفريع في الرد على النصارى ]
وإذا عرفت استحالة افتقاره إلى محل ، واتحاده به. يستحيل قيام صفتِهِ بذات غيرِهِ واتحادها به : فبطل ما قالَتِ النصارى أهلكَهمُ الله : أنَّ أُقنوم الكلمةِ اتَّحد بناسوتِ عيسى عليهِ السلام ) ، واختلفوا في معنى الاتحادِ ؛ فمنهم مَنْ قالَ : إِنَّ الاتحاد يرجع إلى قيامها بهِ كما يقومُ العرضُ بالجوهر ، وهذا يُوجِبُ مفارقته لذات الجوهر الذي هو مجموع الأقانيم الثلاثةِ عندهم ؛ ضرورةً أنَّ المعنى الواحد لا يقوم بذاتين ، فيكونُ الباقي بعض الإله لا إلهاً ، وعيسى أيضاً قامَ بهِ بعضُ الإله ، فلا يكون إلهاً ، فقد لزم على مذهبهم عدم الإله
A subsection on refuting the Christians. And if you know the impossibility of His being in need of a locus and of His union with it, then it is impossible for His attribute to subsist in another essence and for it to unite with it. Thus what the Christians said is false — may God destroy them — that the hypostasis (uqnum) of the Word united with the humanity (nasut) of Jesus, peace be upon him. And they differed on the meaning of union; some of them said that union is reducible to its subsisting in him as the accident subsists in the substance. This necessitates its separation from the essence of the substance, which is the aggregate of the three hypostases according to them, necessarily because one meaning cannot subsist in two essences. Then the remaining part is some of the God, not a god, and Jesus also had some of the God subsisting in him, so he is not a god. Thus their doctrine entails the non-existence of God.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage applies the previous proof of the impossibility of union to refute the Christian claim that the hypostasis of the Word united with the humanity of Jesus. The author first establishes the conclusion: since God cannot be in need of a locus or unite, His attribute cannot subsist in another essence. The Christians claim that the Word (one of the three hypostases) united with Jesus' human nature. They differed on the meaning of union; some said it is like an accident inhering in a substance. If that is the case, then the Word must separate from the divine essence (the substance, which is the aggregate of the three hypostases), because one meaning cannot subsist in two essences. Consequently, the remaining part of the Godhead (after the Word separated) is only a part of God, not God, and Jesus also only has a part of God, so he is not God. Thus their doctrine leads to the non-existence of God entirely.

أقنوم
ناسوت
عرض
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Union means the Word becomes part of Jesus without affecting the Godhead.
Correction: The author shows that if union is like an accident in a substance, the Word must separate from the divine essence, leaving the Godhead incomplete.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the consequence of the Christian view that the Word united with Jesus like an accident in a substance?
A) Jesus becomes God
B) The Word remains in both the divine essence and Jesus
C) The Word leaves the divine essence, making the remaining part not God
D) The Word is annihilated

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that the Christian doctrine of incarnation, when analyzed logically, leads to the destruction of the concept of God itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that leads to a contradictory conclusion, do you have the courage to abandon it, or do you cling to it despite reason? This lesson shows that even a sincere belief can destroy the very truth it claims to uphold. What belief in your own life have you been avoiding examining because you fear what you might find?
?
Sanusi uses logic to defend God's unity. If your understanding of Allah ever contradicts His perfection, will you change your understanding or rationalize? When you read the Quran, are you willing to accept that your interpretation might be wrong if it leads to a conclusion that undermines tawhid?

Transfer and Specification Arguments
Sanusi presents two objections: transfer of attribute and specification to Word.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's arguments that the transfer of an attribute is impossible and that the specification of the union to the Word requires a specifying agent.

Core Idea
Transfer of an essential attribute is impossible, and any specification needs a cause.

Sanusi begins with two pointed objections to the incarnation. He argues that the very idea of transferring an attribute—even for accidental qualities—is absurd, let alone for essential ones. Then he questions why the union is specific to the Word rather than the Holy Spirit or the divine essence, showing that such specificity demands a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two arguments.
2
Understand 'transfer of meaning'
Grasp why transferring an attribute is impossible for accidents and even more so for essentials.
3
Analyze the specification problem
See why the union being specific to the Word requires a specifying agent (mukhassis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه أيضاً : القولُ بانتقالِ المعنى ، وهو محال على الصفاتِ العرضيَّةِ ، فكيف بما هو نفسي عندهم ؟!
وأيضاً : فاختصاص الاتحاد بأقنوم الكلمة دون روح القدس الذي هو أقنوم الحياة ، بل ودونَ الجوهر نفسِهِ.. يحتاج إلى مُخصص . وأيضاً : فالاتحاد إنْ كانَ واجباً لزمَ قِدَمُ الناسوتِ ، وإنْ كانَ جائزاً افتقر إلى مُخصص ، ويلزمُ منهُ جواز زوالِهِ ، فتكون ألوهية عيسى جائزةً ، وذلك يفضي إلى مثلِهِ في واجب الوجود ، وهو محال . وأيضاً : الاتحاد إمَّا أنْ يكونَ وصفَ كمال فيجب للذاتِ الأزليَّةِ أزلاً ، وإنْ كانَ صفة ذمّ فقد وصفوه بالنقائص
الدليل يلزم طردُهُ لا عكشة ]
وأيضاً : يطالبون بتخصيص ناسوت عيسى بهذا الاتحادِ دونَ غيره ، فإن قالوا : وجه الاختصاص ما ظهر على يدِهِ مِنْ إحياء الموتى ونحوِه .. وَرَدَ عليهم ما ظهر على يد موسى عليه السلامُ مِنْ إحياء العصا ثعباناً ونحوه ، بل ويلزمهم أن يُجوزوا اتحاد الكلمة بكل حادثٍ حتى الخنافس والحشراتِ ؛ لأنَّ قُصارى ما انعدم منها على أصلهم
And also in it: the saying of the transfer of the meaning, and it is impossible for accidental attributes, so how about what is essential according to them? And also: the specification of the union to the hypostasis of the Word to the exclusion of the Holy Spirit, who is the hypostasis of life, nay even to the exclusion of the essence itself, requires a specifying agent. And also: if the union is necessary, then the humanity would be eternal, and if it is contingent, it needs a specifying agent, and it entails the possibility of its cessation, so the divinity of Jesus would be contingent, and that leads to the like of it in the Necessary Existent, which is impossible. And also: the union is either a description of perfection, then it would be necessary for the eternal essence eternally, or it is a description of defect, then they have described Him with imperfections. The proof requires its application not its reversal. And also: they are asked to specify the humanity of Jesus with this union to the exclusion of others. If they say: the aspect of specification is what appeared at his hands of reviving the dead and the like... there is refuted upon them what appeared at the hands of Moses, peace be upon him, of turning the staff into a snake and the like. Rather, they are compelled to allow the union of the Word with every contingent, even beetles and insects, because the extent of what is negated from them according to their principle...
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents several additional arguments against the Christian doctrine of the incarnation. (1) The claim of transfer (intiqal) of an attribute is impossible even for accidental attributes, so how could it be for an essential attribute? (2) The specification of the union to the Word alone (not the Holy Spirit or the essence) requires a specifying agent (mukhassis) — meaning it is contingent, not necessary. (3) If the union is necessary, then the humanity of Jesus would be eternal (which is absurd). If it is contingent, it needs a specifying agent and can cease, making Jesus' divinity contingent — and this leads to similar contingency in the Necessary Existent, which is impossible. (4) The union is either a perfection — then it should have been eternal for the divine essence — or a defect — then they attribute defects to God. (5) Finally, they cannot justify why the union is specific to Jesus' humanity, since other prophets performed similar miracles (e.g., Moses' staff), so they would have to allow union with any contingent, including insects, which is absurd. The argument from miracles is thus rejected.

Key Terms
انتقال المعنى
صفات عرضية
تخصص
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that transferring an attribute is possible if God wills it.
Correction: Sanusi argues that transfer is logically impossible, not just religiously prohibited.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the problem with the union being specific to the Word?
A) It shows the union is accidental.
B) It requires a specifying agent.
C) It contradicts the Bible.
D) It makes Jesus divine.

Key Takeaway
The transfer of an attribute is impossible, and any specification requires a cause, undermining the incarnation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's attributes—like His mercy, anger, or justice—are inseparable from His essence? Or do you sometimes, in your daily life, think of them as qualities that could be 'transferred' to you or others? How does this lesson challenge that tendency?
?
The specification argument shows that if something is specific, it needs a cause. In your own life, do you live with the conviction that everything—even your circumstances—is caused by God's wisdom? Or do you sometimes assume things happen by chance? What would change if you truly internalized that nothing is arbitrary?
?
If the incarnation is impossible because attributes cannot transfer and specification requires a cause, what does that demand of you when you next encounter a claim about God that seems convenient or comfortable? Are you willing to weigh it against rigorous logic, or do you prefer mystery without accountability?

Necessity/Contingency and Perfection/Defect Arguments
Sanusi presents two dilemmas: necessity/contingency and perfection/defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the incarnation leads either to the eternity of humanity or to contingency, and why it cannot be a perfection or a defect.

Core Idea
Union must be necessary or contingent—both lead to absurdities; perfection or defect are impossible for God.

Sanusi now presents a dilemma: if the union is necessary, then Jesus' humanity would be eternal; if contingent, it can cease and needs a cause. He then argues that the union is either a perfection—then it should have been eternal for God—or a defect—then God would be imperfect. Both are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the two dilemmas.
2
Analyze the necessity/contingency dilemma
Understand why necessary union implies eternal humanity, and contingent union implies possibility of cessation and the need for a specifying agent.
3
Analyze the perfection/defect dilemma
See why union as perfection must be eternal, and as defect is unworthy of God.
4
Note the marginal line
The line 'الدليل يلزم طردُهُ لا عكشة' is a marginal note about logical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

What would it mean for you to anchor your deen entirely on God alone, as this passage demands? Identify one shaky plank in your spiritual life and commit to replacing it with reliance on Al-Samad.

Miracles and Specificity Argument
Sanusi refutes the idea that miracles prove the incarnation and shows it leads to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why miracles do not prove the incarnation and why the union cannot be specific to Jesus.

Core Idea
Miracles do not prove divine union, as other prophets performed them; otherwise union would apply to insects.

Sanusi's final argument challenges the Christian claim that Jesus' miracles prove his union with God. He points out that Moses also performed miracles, so the same logic would force them to accept union with any being that performs miracles, even beetles and insects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the argument.
2
Understand the refutation
See how Sanusi refutes the miracle-based specificity by citing Moses' miracles.
3
Consider the reductio ad absurdum
Notice the conclusion that if miracles prove union, then any creature with miracles would be divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a clear sign of God's power in your life, do you ever assume that makes you or someone else special in a way that excludes others? How can you guard against using God's favors to justify pride or false beliefs?
?
Sanusi exposes the absurdity of limiting God's miracles to one person. Are there areas in your faith where you have unconsciously limited God's mercy or guidance to a particular group or method? Will you repent of that and open your heart to His wider signs?
?
If you had to defend the oneness of God against a claim like the incarnation, would your argument be as sharp and uncompromising as Sanusi's? What would it take for you to be willing to call an error absurd for the sake of God's transcendence?
