Two Refutations of Jabriyya: Denial of the Self-Evident and Contradiction of Revelation
Sanusi explains the invalidity of Jabriyya via two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments against Jabriyya: that it denies self-evident necessary knowledge and that it contradicts the Sharia's system of obligation.

Core Idea
Jabriyya is refuted by both reason and revelation.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary unpacks the conclusion that the doctrine of Jabr (determinism) is invalid. After establishing the existence of acquired power and its limited role, Sanusi now shows that Jabriyya not only violates a necessary distinction everyone knows intuitively, but also undermines the very structure of Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation to get a clear sense of the argument.
Notice that the passage begins by linking back to two earlier proofs.
2
Identify the two criticisms
Find where Sanusi mentions 'denial of the necessary' (jahl al-ḍarūra) and 'nullification of the Sharia' (ibṭāl al-sharīʿa). Understand what each refers to.
The first is about a self-evident distinction; the second about religious obligation.
3
Reflect on the severity
Consider why the second criticism is more serious. If Jabriyya only involved ignorance, it would be mere stupidity; but it actually contradicts the Sharia.
The Sharia removes obligation from impossible acts and obliges only feasible ones—this presupposes human capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فبطل إذاً مذهب الجبرِ ...) إلى آخرِهِ: مُسبَّبٌ عمَّا سبقَ قبلُ مِنْ دليلي إثباتِ القدرةِ الحادثة وإبطال تأثيرها في مقدورها، وقد أعاد الدليلين هنا على سبيل الإجمال فقوله: (لما فيهِ مِنْ جَحْدِ الضرورة) أي: الضرورة التي تقدَّمَتْ في الفرق بين حركة الاضطرار والاكتساب. وقوله: (وإبطال) (مخفوض بالعطف على (جحْدِ) يعني: أنَّهُ لو لم يكن في مذهبِ الجبرِ إلا ثبوتُ جهل بأمرٍ يُدرَكُ ضرورةً مِنْ غيرِ مصادمة للشريعة.. لكانَ أمره سهلاً؛ إذْ غاية ما يلزم فيه التناهي في الغباوةِ وضَعْفِ العقلِ، كيف والمذهب مصادم للشريعة؟! لأنها قد جاءَتْ بإسقاط التكليف بالأفعال التي لا يتمكَّنُ العبد فيها عادةً مِنَ الاتصاف بوجودها وعدمها، وبالتكليف بما يتيسر منها على العبدِ
His saying: “So the doctrine of Jabr is invalidated, therefore...” to its end: is a result of what preceded before from the two proofs for establishing the acquired power and nullifying its effect in its predetermined act. And he has repeated the two proofs here in summary. So his saying: “because in it there is denial of the necessary [knowledge]” means: the necessary [knowledge] that preceded concerning the distinction between the involuntary movement and the acquired movement. And his saying: “and nullification” (in the genitive case, coordinated with “denial”) means: that if there were nothing in the doctrine of Jabr except the affirmation of ignorance of a matter known necessarily, without contradicting the Sharia, it would be easy; since the utmost consequence in it would be extreme stupidity and weakness of intellect. How [then] when the doctrine contradicts the Sharia?! Because the Sharia has come with the removal of obligation concerning acts which the servant is not usually able to be described with existence and non-existence, and with obligation concerning what is feasible for the servant among them.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an additional verification in refuting the doctrine of Jabriyya (determinism). The author explains a statement that says 'So the doctrine of Jabr is invalidated' (فبطل إذاً مذهب الجبر) and notes that this conclusion (musabbab) is based on the two earlier proofs: one for establishing the acquired power (qudra ḥāditha) and the other for negating its effect on its predetermined act. These two proofs are summarized here. The author then unpacks two objections against Jabriyya: First, 'denial of the necessary' (جحد الضرورة): this refers to the necessary knowledge that distinguishes between involuntary movements (like tremor) and voluntary acquired movements. Jabriyya denies this self-evident distinction. Second, 'nullification' (إبطال) of the Sharia: if the only problem with Jabriyya were ignorance of a necessary truth without contradicting the Sharia, it would be merely stupidity. However, Jabriyya actually contradicts the Sharia because the Sharia has removed obligation from acts that the servant cannot normally perform or avoid (like involuntary acts) and has placed obligation on acts that are feasible for the servant (i.e., voluntary acts). By denying any role for the servant's power, Jabriyya undermines the very basis of religious obligation.

Key Terms
مذهب الجبر
الضرورة
الفرق بين حركة الاضطرار والاكتساب
إبطال الشريعة
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the only problem with Jabriyya is that it denies human power, but it also contradicts the Sharia.
Correction: Jabriyya is refuted for both denying a self-evident truth and for undermining the entire system of Islamic obligation (taklīf).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two main criticisms of the Jabriyya doctrine?
A) Denial of the necessary and nullification of the Sharia.
B) Denial of Allah's power and rejection of prophethood.
C) Affirmation of human independence and denial of divine will.
D) Just ignorance of necessary truths without any Sharia contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Jabriyya is refuted because it denies the self-evident distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions and contradicts the Islamic concept of obligation based on feasible acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you secretly believe that you have no choice—that circumstances, genetics, or upbringing have already decided your actions? How does this lesson's insistence on the self-evident distinction between voluntary and involuntary movements confront that belief? Will you today identify one such area and consciously act with the intention of taking responsibility?
?
The passage says that if Jabriyya only denied necessary knowledge, it would be 'extreme stupidity and weakness of intellect,' but it also contradicts Sharia. Are there any comfortable beliefs you hold that deny your moral responsibility while still claiming to follow Islam? What would it cost you to honestly examine those beliefs and align them with the Sharia's system of obligation?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'It was meant to be' or 'I couldn't help it'? That is the whisper of Jabriyya in your heart. How will you train yourself to reject that thought and instead embrace the weight of your free will and accountability as taught in this lesson? Write one concrete change you will make today.

Mu'tazilite Disagreement on Tawallud
Objection to claimed Mu'tazilite consensus on tawallud by citing exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud is challenged by internal disagreements, and why claiming consensus is problematic.

Core Idea
Even within the same school, scholars disagreed on whether generated acts belong to the agent or to God.

This lesson presents an objection to the idea that all Mu'tazilites agreed on the nature of generated acts (tawallud). The questioner cites two prominent Mu'tazilites, al-Nazzam and Hafs al-Qird, who held nuanced positions that break the supposed consensus. Examining their views reveals internal diversity and challenges the coherence of the Mu'tazilite stance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim of consensus
Imam al-Haramayn is reported to have said that the Mu'tazilites agreed that the generated act is the act of the agent of the cause.
This claim sets up the objection.
2
Examine al-Nazzam's view
Al-Nazzam attributed generated events to God's creation of natures and properties in bodies, not to human agents.
He did not say they are the act of the agent.
3
Examine Hafs al-Qird's distinction
Hafs al-Qird differentiated: acts that depend on the causer's choice (e.g., cutting) are the agent's act; those that don't (e.g., falling) are not.
This splits the category of generated acts.
4
Reflect on the implication
These examples show internal division, undermining the claim of unanimous agreement among the Mu'tazilites.
The objection is left open, unresolved in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى لرومهم في أصلهم قطع نسبة القبائح إليه ، ومذهبهم في التولدِ يُلْزِمهم ما فرُّوا منهُ مِنْ نسبة فعلها إليه ، وكونُ المتولدِ فعل فاعل السبب .. قد نقل إمام الحرمين في الشامل » اتفاق المعتزلة عليه (۱) )) قال المقترح : ( ولا يصح ؛ فقد ذهب النظَّامُ منهم إلى أنَّ المتولَّداتِ مضافةً إلى البارئ سبحانَهُ لا على معنى أنَّه فعلها (٢) ، ولكن بمعنى أنه خلقَ الأجسام على طبائع وخصائص تقتضي حدوث الحوادثِ الناشئة عنها ، ولم يقل : إنَّها فعل لفاعل سببها . وذهب حفصُ القِردِ (۳) : إلى أنَّ ما يقع مبايناً لمحل القدرة على قدر اختيار المسببِ .. فهو فعلُ فاعل السبب ؛ كالقطع والفصدِ والذبح ، وما لا يقفُ على قدرِ اختيار المسبِّبِ ؛ كالهوي عند الاندفاع ونحوه .. فليسَ مِنْ فعلِهِ
…high exalted [God] in their origin cutting off attribution of ugly acts to Him, and their doctrine of generation (tawallud) obliges them what they fled from of attributing the act to Him, and that the generated act is the act of the agent of the cause.. Imam al-Haramayn has transmitted in al-Shamil the agreement of the Mu'tazilites upon it (1). The questioner said: (It is not valid; for al-Nazzam among them held that generated things are attributed to the Creator, Exalted is He, not in the sense that He did them (2), but in the sense that He created bodies with natures and properties that necessitate the occurrence of events arising from them, and he did not say: they are the act of the agent of their cause. And Hafs al-Qird (3) held: that what occurs separately from the locus of power, according to the capacity of the choice of the causer... it is the act of the agent of the cause; like cutting, bloodletting, and slaughtering, and what does not depend on the capacity of the choice of the causer; like falling when pushed and the like... it is not from his act.)
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the claim that all Mu'tazilites agreed on the theory of generated acts (tawallud). The author first quotes Imam al-Haramayn stating that the Mu'tazilites agreed that the generated act is the act of the agent of the cause. Then the questioner (muqtarih) objects, saying this is not correct, because al-Nazzam held a different view: he attributed generated events to God's creation of bodies with inherent natures, not to the agent of the cause. Additionally, Hafs al-Qird distinguished between acts that depend on the causer's choice (like cutting) which are the act of the agent of the cause, and acts that do not (like falling) which are not. Thus the questioner uses these two counterexamples to show that the claimed agreement is false. The passage ends without a direct refutation, leaving the objection open.

Key Terms
التولد (tawallud)
المتولد (al-mutawallad)
فاعل السبب (fa'il al-sabab)
النظام (al-Nazzam)
حفص القرد (Hafs al-Qird)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites were unanimous about generated acts being the act of the agent.
Correction: Al-Nazzam and Hafs al-Qird held alternative views, breaking the consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the questioner, what is the main problem with claiming that all Mu'tazilites agreed on tawallud?
A) They did not discuss tawallud at all.
B) Al-Nazzam and Hafs al-Qird held different opinions that break the alleged consensus.
C) Imam al-Haramayn misquoted them.
D) The questioner agrees with the consensus.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite school was not monolithic on tawallud; internal disagreements challenge claims of consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that even within the same school, scholars disagreed on a fundamental issue like how our actions relate to God. How do you react when you encounter disagreements among scholars? Do you use them as an excuse to dismiss the truth, or do they motivate you to seek deeper understanding? Challenge yourself to identify one area where you have used scholarly disagreement to avoid personal accountability, and commit to investigating it further this week.
?
The Mu'tazilites were accused of inconsistency: they tried to protect God's transcendence but ended up attributing ugly acts to Him. In your own life, have you ever held a position that seemed noble but actually led to consequences you didn't intend? How can you humble yourself to reconsider your views in light of the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The questioner in the passage uses specific examples to refute a claim of consensus. When you are faced with a claim that everyone agrees on something, do you blindly accept it, or do you examine the evidence? Think of a belief you hold because 'everyone says so'. Is it truly from revelation? What would it cost you to verify it?

Refutation of the Ray Theory of Vision: The Pupil-Size Argument
Sanusi refutes the emission theory of vision with the pupil-size argument and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the emission theory of vision using the pupil-size argument, and how the Mu'tazila's response is refuted.

Core Idea
Pupil size refutes emission theory: you see more than your pupil can emit.

This passage presents a tight dialectical exchange between the proponents of the emission theory of vision (the Mu'tazila) and their critics. Sanusi shows that if vision were by rays leaving the eye, the pupil would limit what we see, yet we see entire mountains at once. The Mu'tazila answer with the luminous air analogy, which Sanusi then dismantles by pointing out that we still see in darkness and that we see more of the air itself than the pupil permits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the dialectical structure: objection, response, rebuttal.
2
Identify the initial objection
Find the initial claim that if vision were by emission of rays, one would only see as much as the pupil.
This is the core logical flaw Sanusi exposes.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila response
Notice how the Mu'tazila deny the necessary connection by invoking luminous air and the analogy of crystal.
They argue that the emitted rays connect with luminous air to extend vision.
4
Understand the refutation
See how Sanusi responds with two counter-arguments: the case of darkness and the observation that we see more of the air itself.
These rebuttals show the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila's defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وذلك لو صح لوجبَ ... ( إلى آخرِهِ : هذا مِنْ جملة ما رُدَّ بهِ عليهمُ القولُ بانبعاث الأشعة ؛ وهو أنَّه لو كانَتِ الرؤية بانبعاث الأشعة لزمَ ألا يرى الإنسانُ إلا قدْرَ حدقتِهِ ؛ إِذْ لا تسع حدقتُهُ مِنَ الأشعة أكثر منها ، لكنَّهُ يرى دفعةً أكثرَ مِنْ ذاتِهِ كلَّها بأضعافِ عن حدقتِهِ ، فدل على أنَّ الرؤية ليسَتْ بما يزعمونَ من انبعاث الأشعة . قالوا : إنَّما ذلكَ لاتصال الشعاع بالهواء وهو مضيء ، فأعانَ على رؤية ما قابله ، كالبلورِ المُعِينِ بإشراقه على رؤية ما فيه . قلنا : فيلزمُ أَلا يَرى مِنَ الهواء إلا قدر حدقتِهِ ، وأيضاً : فنحن نرى والهواء مظلم ما نراه والهواء مشرق . يعني : أنَّهم أجابوا عمَّا أُلْزِموهُ مِنْ عدم رؤية الإنسان أكثرَ مِنْ حدقتِهِ بأن منعوا الملازمةَ ، ومستندهم : أَنَّهُ إِنَّما رأى الكثير لأنَّ أجزاء الهواء مضيئة ، فيتصلُ الشعاعُ بها وهي تتصل بالسماءِ ، فتعين على الإبصار ، كما أنَّ البلور إذا اتَّصلَ الشعاعُ به وهو جسم مضيء متصل بما فيه فيُرى ما فيه . ورُدَّ عليهم : بأنَّهُ لو كانَ كذلكَ لزمَ ألا يُرى الكثيرُ مِنَ السماءِ وغيرها حيثُ يكون الهواء مظلماً بالليل مثلاً ، وأيضاً : فما باله رأى مِنَ الهواء نفسِهِ أكثرَ مِنْ حدقتِهِ معَ أَنَّ الشعاعَ إِنَّمَا اتَّصلَ ببعضه ؟!
His statement: (And that if it were correct, it would be necessary ...) to its end: This is part of what was used to refute their saying about the emission of rays; that is, if vision were by emission of rays, it would necessarily follow that a man would see only as much as the size of his pupil, since his pupil cannot contain more rays than that. But he sees at once more than his entire self, many multiples of his pupil. So this indicates that vision is not by what they claim of emission of rays. They said: That is only due to the connection of the ray with the air, which is luminous, so it helps to see what faces it, like crystal that helps by its illumination to see what is in it. We said: Then it would necessarily follow that one would not see of the air except the size of his pupil. Also: We see when the air is dark as well as when the air is bright. That is: They responded to the objection they were faced with of man not seeing more than his pupil by denying the necessary connection, and their basis: that he only sees much because the parts of the air are luminous, so the ray connects to them and they connect to the sky, thus aiding sight, just as when the ray connects to crystal, which is a luminous body connected to what is in it, so what is in it is seen. And a rebuttal was made against them: that if it were so, it would necessarily follow that much of the sky and other things would not be seen when the air is dark, e.g., at night. Also: Why does he see of the air itself more than his pupil, while the ray only connected to some of it?!
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical refutation of the Mu'tazilite theory that vision occurs by the emission of rays from the eye. The commentator begins by quoting an earlier argument: if vision were by emission of rays, then the eye would only see as much as the size of its pupil, because the pupil cannot emit more rays than it contains. Yet we see objects much larger than our pupil at once. This disproves the theory. The Mu'tazila respond by claiming that the emitted rays connect with the luminous air, which then helps to see larger objects, analogous to how a crystal helps to see things inside it when illuminated. The commentator then rebuts: first, if that were the case, then one would still not see more of the air itself than the pupil size; second, we see even when the air is dark (e.g., at night), which contradicts the need for luminous air. The commentator further explains the Mu'tazila's answer in more detail: they denied the necessary connection (that pupil size limits vision) by claiming that the rays connect with luminous air particles that in turn connect to the sky, thereby extending the field of view. The rebuttal reiterates that in darkness this mechanism fails, and also asks why one sees more of the air itself than the pupil, given that the ray only connects to a portion of it. The argument flows logically: initial objection, Mu'tazila answer, then a two-part counter-rebuttal that exposes the inconsistency.

Key Terms
انبعاث الأشعة (emission of rays)
الشعاع (ray)
الحدقة (pupil)
الهواء (air)
البلور (crystal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila's luminous air argument successfully explains how we see larger objects despite the pupil limit.
Correction: Sanusi refutes it by pointing out that in darkness, when air is not luminous, we still see large objects, and that we see more of the air itself than the pupil size, contradicting the necessity of luminous air.

Checkpoint
What is the initial objection against the ray theory of vision presented in this passage?
A) The eye cannot see in the dark.
B) The pupil is too small to emit enough rays to see large objects.
C) The air is not luminous.
D) The eye does not emit rays at all.

Key Takeaway
The pupil-size argument shows that vision cannot be by emission of rays from the eye, because we see objects much larger than our pupil, and the luminous air defense fails against darkness and the fact that we see more air than the pupil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the Mu'tazila presented their luminous air argument, it must have seemed plausible at first. Have you ever accepted a belief because it was cleverly argued, only to later realize it contradicted simple evidence you already had? What was that evidence, and why did you overlook it?
?
The rebuttal uses two simple observations: we see in darkness and we see more of the air than the pupil. These are ordinary facts that anyone can check. Are there basic truths from the Qur'an or about your own life that you are ignoring because you are attached to a more complex explanation? Will you start testing your beliefs against the simplest facts today?
?
The Mu'tazila analogized vision to crystal lit by a lamp. How often do you rely on analogies to defend your opinions? Are you willing to let your analogies be dismantled by straightforward counterexamples, or do you cling to them? What analogy in your life might be hiding a flaw you need to see?

The Indivisible Atom Refutes the Emission Theory
Sanusi presents the indivisible atom argument against the emission theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the invisibility of the indivisible atom undermines the Mu'tazilite theory of vision requiring ray connection.

Core Idea
If ray connection causes visibility, atoms should be seen—but they are not.

Sanusi presents a clever logical contradiction. According to the emission theory, any object that receives a ray from the eye becomes visible. The indivisible atom, when alone, receives the same ray as when it is part of a larger body. Yet we do not see atoms. This shows something is wrong with the theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice that Sanusi first states the refutation and then explains the reasoning.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the condition and the observation
The condition: the ray connects to the atom when alone, just as when together. The observation: the atom is not seen when alone. Ask yourself why this is a contradiction for the theory.
The theory says visibility follows from ray connection.
3
Understand why this refutes the theory
The theory claims that ray connection is sufficient for visibility. Here, the condition is met (ray connects) but visibility does not occur. Therefore, the theory's claim is false.
This is a direct counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما ينقضُ عليهم : عدم رؤيةِ الجوهر الفردِ معَ اتصال الشعاع بهِ ، ولا ينالُهُ مِنْ ذلك وحدَهُ إِلا ما ينالُهُ معَ غيرِهِ ، وكذا رؤية الكبير مع البعدِ صغيراً معَ اتصال الشعاع والمقابلة لجميعه . يعني أنَّهُ ممَّا ينقصُ عليهم ادعاءَهم وجوبَ رؤيةِ مَا اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاع . . الجوهر الفردُ إذا كانَ في سمْتِ الشعاع ؛ فإنَّهُ لا مانع على زعمهم من اتصال الشعاع به ، بدليل أنَّها تتصل بهِ عندَ اجتماعِهِ معَ غيره ، ولا ينالُهُ مِنَ الشعاعِ عند الاتصال إلا ما يناله عند الانفصال ، فكانَ يجب على قولهم أَنْ يُرى عند الانفرادِ معَ أَنَّهُ لا يُرى . وكذا ينتقص مذهبهم برؤية الكبير مع البعد صغيراً ، مع أن شرط
And among what rebuts them: the non-vision of the indivisible atom despite the connection of the ray to it, and it receives from that alone only what it receives when with other things, and likewise the seeing of the large at a distance as small despite the connection of the ray and its facing all of it. That is: it is among what rebuts their claim of the necessity of seeing whatever the ray connects to... The indivisible atom, if it is in the direction of the ray, then there is no obstacle, according to their claim, for the ray to connect to it, as evidenced by the fact that it connects to it when it is together with others. But it receives from the ray upon connection only what it receives when separated. So it should, according to their saying, be seen when alone, yet it is not seen. Likewise, their school is refuted by the seeing of the large at a distance as small, despite the condition
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage presents two further refutations of the Mu'tazilite emission theory. First, the case of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). According to the theory, if a ray connects to an object, that object must be visible. The atom, when alone, should be visible because the ray can connect to it just as it does when the atom is part of a larger body. But in fact, we do not see individual atoms. Therefore, the theory is false. The commentator explains that the atom receives the same amount of ray whether alone or combined, so there is no reason for it to become invisible when alone. The second refutation concerns the perception of size: a large object at a distance appears small, even though the ray connects to the entire object and faces it. According to the theory, the object should appear its full size, since the ray covers all of it. But it does not. The passage ends abruptly with 'despite the condition', but the argument is clear: the emission theory cannot explain why distant large objects appear small. These arguments demonstrate the internal inconsistency of the Mu'tazila's claim that vision requires the ray to connect with the object seen.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد (indivisible atom)
الشعاع (ray)
اتصال (connection)
الانفراد (being alone)
الاجتماع (being together)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the atom's invisibility is due to its small size, making it imperceptible.
Correction: The argument is not about size but about the theory's condition: ray connection is met, so the theory predicts visibility. The fact that we do not see atoms shows the theory's condition is insufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why should the indivisible atom be visible according to the Mu'tazila's theory?
A) Because it is always in the direction of the ray.
B) Because the ray connects to it just as it does when it is with other atoms.
C) Because it is very small.
D) Because the air is luminous.

Key Takeaway
The indivisible atom's invisibility, despite satisfying the ray connection condition, disproves the Mu'tazilite theory that ray connection is sufficient for vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any belief about how you perceive the world that seems obvious but has never been critically examined? What evidence would it take to change your mind?
?
This lesson shows that a seemingly logical theory can be disproven by a single counterexample. When was the last time you admitted that a long-held assumption of yours was wrong? What did that cost you?
?
If the invisibility of the indivisible atom can overturn an entire theory of vision, what uncomfortable truths about your own faith or worldview might you be ignoring because they don't fit your comfortable framework? Will you actively search for such contradictions today?

Why Distant Objects Appear Small Challenges the Emission Theory
Sanusi presents the size perception argument against the emission theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent shrinking of distant objects refutes the Mu'tazilite theory of vision.

Core Idea
If the ray covers the whole object, it should appear full size—but it doesn't.

The second refutation concerns a common experience: a large object far away looks small. According to the emission theory, if the ray connects to the entire object and faces it, the object should appear its actual size. But it appears small. This inconsistency further disproves the theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage. Notice that Sanusi introduces the size argument after the atom argument. The text is incomplete but the argument is clear.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the intended meaning is preserved.
2
Identify the condition and the observation
Condition: the ray connects to the entirety of a large distant object and faces it. Observation: the object appears small, not large. Ask yourself why this contradicts the theory.
The theory would predict that the object appears its actual size.
3
Understand why this refutes the theory
If ray connection determines perceived size, then a fully illuminated distant object should appear its full size. But experience shows it appears small. Therefore, the theory's link between ray connection and perceived size is false.
This is another counterexample to the emission theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage presents two further refutations of the Mu'tazilite emission theory. First, the case of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). According to the theory, if a ray connects to an object, that object must be visible. The atom, when alone, should be visible because the ray can connect to it just as it does when the atom is part of a larger body. But in fact, we do not see individual atoms. Key Terms
الكبير مع البعد (large with distance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that distance makes objects physically smaller, so the theory is not challenged.
Correction: The theory is about perception, not physical size. The object remains large physically, but its apparent size changes. The theory cannot explain why apparent size changes despite full ray connection.

Checkpoint

A) A small object at a distance appears large.

The emission theory fails to explain why a large distant object appears small despite the ray fully connecting to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever cling to a worldview or opinion despite clear evidence that contradicts it? This passage shows that a school of thought collapsed under one contradictory observation. Where in your own life has experience contradicted your assumptions, and have you been willing to change?
?
The Mu'tazilites believed the eye emits a ray that touches objects. Sanusi points out that this fails to explain why distant large objects appear small. Think of something you have always believed about yourself or others that 'should' be true but does not match reality. Are you willing to accept that your 'ray' might be flawed, or will you twist reality to fit your theory?
?
This argument is a reminder that our theories about how the world works must correspond to what we actually observe. Have you ever ignored a plain fact because it didn't fit your preferred narrative? Is there a fact you are ignoring right now? What would it cost you to admit it?

Opponents' Geometric Vision Argument and Initial Rebuttal
Opponents' vision claim and initial rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' geometric model of vision and the initial refutation by Ahl al-Haqq based on observational contradiction.

Core Idea
If distance makes edges invisible, moving the whole object should make it vanish.

This lesson introduces a debate about how vision works. The opponents propose that rays from the eye create a triangle with the visible object, and the center is seen more clearly than the edges. Ahl al-Haqq respond with a simple logical test: if the object moves to the distance of the edges, it should become invisible, but it does not.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' claim
Read the first sentences describing how the ray forms an acute angle and stands perpendicular to the base. Note the claim that the center line is shortest.
The term 'الجميع' is unclear but the geometric idea is clear.
2
Identify the initial rebuttal
Find where Ahl al-Haqq respond with 'قلنا'. They argue that if the object moves to the distance of the sides, it should not be seen, contradicting observation.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider why this refutation is powerful: it uses the opponents' own assumption to derive a false conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرؤية على زعمهم موجود؛ وهو اتصال الشعاع والمقابلة الجميع[؟]. قالوا: إنما ذلك لأن الشعاع نفذ من زاوية حادة لمثلث قاعدته المرئي، فقام خطاً مستقيماً بوسط القاعدة على زوايا قائمة، ومعلوم أنه أصغر مما يقوم عليها من سائر الخطوط، فزيادة ذلك البعد لغيره منعت من رؤية طرفي المرئي. قلنا: فيلزم إذا انتقل المرئي إلى مقدار تلك الزيادة من البعد ألا يُرى، والمشاهدة تكذبه. يعني: أنهم أجابوا عما نقض عليهم من رؤية الكبير صغيراً بأن قالوا: لا نسلم استواء نسبة أجزاء الكبير مع البعد إلى الرائي حتى يلزم أن يراه على حاله كبيراً؛ وذلك لأن الجزء الواقع في وسط المرئي أقرب إلى الناظر من الجزء الواقع في طرفه. وبيانه: أنه إذا خرج خطان شعاعيان موهومان كساقي مثلث، ونفرض أن قاعدة هذا المثلث - أي: الخط الذي يقوم عليه ذانك الساقان - جسم المرئي البعيد، فيكون هذان الساقان على طرفيه، وخرج من نقطة العين خط آخر قسم ذلك المثلث بنصفين، وقام بوسط تلك القاعدة؛ فإنه تحدث فيه زاويتان قائمتان، ويكون كل واحد من الخطين الواقعين على الطرفين وتراً للزاوية القائمة، وقد تبين في الهندسة أن وتر الزاوية القائمة التي في المثلث أطول من كل واحد من الخطين المحيطين بها، فالخطان الواقعان على الطرفين أطول من الخط الواقع على وسط الجسم المرئي، فتكون الأجزاء التي وقع عليها الطرفان أبعد عن البصر من الأجزاء التي يقع عليها الخط الأوسط، فنسبة الأجزاء إذاً ليست متساوية في القرب والبعد، فلذلك صح أن يُرى بعض الجسم دون بعض، فيُرى الكبير صغيراً. وهذه صورة المثلث: الرائي، الوتر الأيمن، قاعدة المرئي، الوتر الأيسر. أجابهم أهل الحق رضي الله تعالى عنهم: بأنه إذا كان البعد الحاصل بين المرئي والناظر مئة ذراع مثلاً، والذي بين طرفيه زائداً على المئة قدر ذراع .. فكان يجب - إذا انتقل ذلك الجسم الكبير الذي رئي صغيراً إلى مسافة الطرفين؛ وهي مئة ذراع وذراع - ألا يُرى ألبتة كما لم يُر الطرفان الكائنان في تلك المسافة، لكن المشاهدة تكذب ذلك، فبطل ما ذكروه.
Vision, according to their claim, exists; and it is the connection of the ray and the facing of the whole[?]. They said: This is only because the ray penetrates from an acute angle of a triangle whose base is the visible object, so it stands as a straight line at the middle of the base at right angles, and it is known that it is smaller than all the lines that stand upon it, so the increase of that distance for something else prevented the seeing of the two sides of the visible object. We said: Then it follows that if the visible object moves to the amount of that increase in distance, it should not be seen, and observation falsifies it. Meaning: They responded to what had been rebutted against them about seeing a large object as small by saying: We do not concede the equality of the proportion of the parts of the large object with distance to the viewer such that it would necessarily be seen as large; and that is because the part lying in the middle of the visible object is closer to the observer than the part lying at its edge. Its explanation: If two imaginary ray-lines come out like the two legs of a triangle, and we suppose that the base of this triangle – i.e., the line upon which those two legs stand – is the body of the distant visible object, then these two legs are at its two sides, and another line comes out from the point of the eye that divides that triangle into two halves and stands at the middle of that base; then two right angles occur in it, and each of the two lines falling at the two sides becomes the hypotenuse of the right angle. It has been shown in geometry that the hypotenuse of the right angle in the triangle is longer than each of the two lines surrounding it. So the two lines falling at the two sides are longer than the line falling at the middle of the visible body. Thus the parts on which the two sides fall are farther from the eye than the parts on which the middle line falls. Therefore the proportion of the parts is not equal in nearness and distance, so it is valid that some of the body is seen without others, so the large is seen as small. And this is a picture of the triangle: the viewer, the right hypotenuse, the base of the visible object, the left hypotenuse. The people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq), may Allah be pleased with them, responded: If the distance between the visible object and the viewer is, for example, one hundred cubits, and the distance between its two sides is one hundred cubits plus a cubit, then it would be necessary – if that large object which was seen as small moved to the distance of the two sides, which is one hundred cubits and a cubit – that it should not be seen at all, just as the two sides which were at that distance were not seen. But observation falsifies that, so what they mentioned is invalidated.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer argument concerning the physical explanation of vision. It begins by stating the opponents' position: they claim that vision occurs via rays that emanate from the eye to the object. They explain that when a large object is seen from a distance, it appears small because the ray hitting the center is perpendicular and shorter, while the rays to the edges are longer, making the edges seem farther. The author then records the initial response of Ahl al-Haqq: if the object moves to the distance of the edges, it should become completely invisible, contradicting observation. Next, the opponents are given a chance to elaborate their answer via the word 'يعني', where they deny that the parts of the object are equally distant. They construct a geometric model: two rays from the eye to the edges form a triangle, with a third ray to the center splitting it into two right triangles. Since the hypotenuse is longer than the legs, the edges are farther, so the object appears smaller. Finally, the decisive refutation from Ahl al-Haqq is presented: using a concrete numerical example (100 cubits to center, 101 to edges), they argue that if the entire object were moved to 101 cubits, it should vanish, but it does not. Therefore, the geometric explanation fails because it implies an absurd consequence. The passage thus demonstrates how a logical reductio ad absurdum can refute a flawed physical theory.

Key Terms
شعاع
مرئي
زاوية حادة
قاعدة
مشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponents' argument is obviously true because large objects appear small.
Correction: The argument relies on an assumption that edges are farther, but it leads to a contradiction when the entire object moves to that distance.

Checkpoint
What is the initial refutation of the opponents' geometric explanation?
A) The ray cannot be straight.
B) If the object moves to the distance of the sides, it should become invisible, but it does not.
C) The triangle is not a right triangle.
D) The object is actually closer than the sides.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' geometric model leads to a self-contradiction when tested with object movement.

هل سبق أن تمسكت برأي أو نظرية بدت لك منطقية، ولكن عندما وضعتها تحت اختبار الواقع تبين خطؤها؟ هل أنت مستعد لتتخلى عن آرائك عندما يكذبها الواقع، حتى لو كانت مريحة لك؟

استخدم الخصوم الهندسة لتفسير ظاهرة يومية. هل بنيتَ يوماً تفسيراً معقداً لأمر في حياتك ظننته صائباً، لكنه لم يصمد أمام التدقيق؟ ماذا يتطلب منك الأمر لتعترف بأن تفسيرك كان خاطئاً؟

Opponents' Detailed Geometric Model and Final Refutation
Detailed geometric model and decisive refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' detailed geometric model of vision using right triangles and the conclusive refutation by Ahl al-Haqq using a numerical example.

Core Idea
Geometry explains why edges are farther, but shifting the entire object disproves it.

In response to the initial rebuttal, the opponents elaborate their geometric model with a right triangle diagram, showing that the edges are farther from the eye than the center. Ahl al-Haqq then deliver the final blow: using concrete numbers, they demonstrate that the model would imply invisibility at the edge distance, which is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' detailed explanation
Read from 'يعني' to the diagram description. Understand the triangle with two legs and a middle line creating right angles.
This is a classic geometric optics argument.
2
Identify the geometric conclusion
The opponents conclude that the proportions are not equal, so some parts are not seen, making the object appear small.
They reference Euclidean geometry about hypotenuses.
3
Study the final refutation
Read the response of Ahl al-Haqq. They use a numerical example: if the center is at 100 cubits and edges at 101, moving the whole object to 101 should make it invisible, but it is still seen.
This invalidates the model.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice the structure: the opponents refine their argument, but Ahl al-Haqq apply the same logical test more thoroughly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Mu'tazilite Obligatory Grace Refuted
Sanusi defines the Mu'tazilite concept of obligatory grace and quotes a hadith against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite doctrine of obligatory grace and the Ash'ari refutation based on a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites claimed God must do what is best; the Prophet called them adversaries of God.

The Mu'tazilites held that God is obligated to provide grace and remove impediments to obedience, a view the Ash'aris reject as limiting God's absolute sovereignty. This passage defines their position and then refutes it using a powerful hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi defines the Mu'tazilite view in detail before refuting it.
2
Identify the four obligations
The passage lists four things the Mu'tazilites obligate upon God: (1) consideration of what is most beneficial, (2) grace, (3) completion of intellect, (4) enabling and removing impediments. List them as you read.
3
Understand the consequence
The Mu'tazilites claim that if God failed to do these, they would have a dispute with Him. Reflect on the audacity of this claim.
4
Reflect on the hadith
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The Qadariyya are the adversaries of God in destiny.' Understand why this hadith refutes the Mu'tazilite position.
The hadith shows that such a view is a form of disputing God's decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ حدُّ اللطفِ الواجب الذي قالَتْ بهِ المعتزلة ]
وخالفهمُ المعتزلة ؛ فأوجبوا مراعاة الأصلح للعباد ، وأوجبوا اللطف ؛ وهو خلْقُ الشيء الذي يُوجِبُ للمُكلَّف ترجيح جانبِ الطاعةِ مِنْ غيرِ أنْ ينتهي إلى حدّ الإلجاء ، وأوجبوا إكمال عقل مَنْ أَرادَ تكليفه ، وإقدارَهُ ، وإزاحة العلل عنه التي تمنعُهُ مِنْ أَداءِ مَا كُلَّفَ بِهِ ، حتى إنَّهُ لو أَخَلَّ بذلك لكانَتْ لهم خصومة له ومطالبة بحقهم ! تعالى الله عمَّا يقولُ الظالمون علوّاً كبيراً .
ولقد صدق فيهم قوله صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : " الْقَدَرِيَّةُ خُصَمَاءُ اللهِ فِي الْقَدَرِ "
[The definition of the obligatory grace (al-lutf al-wājib) which the Mu'tazilites said.] And the Mu'tazilites opposed them [the people of truth]; so they obligated consideration of what is most beneficial for the servants, and they obligated grace (al-lutf), which is the creation of that which causes the morally responsible person to favor the side of obedience without reaching the point of compulsion. And they obligated the completion of the intellect of the one whom He intends to obligate, and enabling him, and removing the impediments from him that prevent him from performing what he is obligated to do. So much so that if He failed to do that, they would have a dispute with Him and a claim of their right! Exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, with great exaltation. And indeed the saying of him – may God bless him and grant him peace – proved true about them: 'The Qadariyya are the adversaries of God in destiny.'
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazilite concept of 'obligatory grace' (al-lutf al-wājib) and refutes it. The author first states that the Mu'tazilites opposed the Ash'aris by making obligatory upon God the consideration of what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for His servants. They also obligated 'grace' (al-lutf), defined as the creation of something that inclines a morally responsible person toward obedience without compulsion. Furthermore, they obligated God to complete the intellect of the one He intends to obligate, to enable him, and to remove impediments that would prevent him from fulfilling his obligations. The author then emphasizes the audacity of this claim: if God failed to do any of this, the Mu'tazilites would have a dispute with God and demand their rights! This is followed by the exclamation: 'Exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say.' Finally, the author cites a hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) called the Qadariyya (those who affirm free will in a way that limits God's power) the 'adversaries of God in destiny,' indicating that this doctrine is a form of disputing God's decree.

Key Terms
اللطف
الأصلح
إكمال العقل
إزاحة العلل
الإلجاء
القدرية
خصماء الله في القدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites believe that God is obligated to provide the most beneficial for His servants and to remove impediments to obedience.
Correction: This passage refutes that by showing it leads to disputing God and is condemned by the hadith.

Checkpoint
What did the Mu'tazilites obligate upon God according to this passage?
A) To create evil actions for the servants.
B) To consider what is most beneficial for the servants and provide grace.
C) To reward all servants equally regardless of their actions.
D) To punish the sinful without mercy.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite view that God must provide grace is refuted by the hadith calling them adversaries of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God owes you something—health, wealth, or a specific outcome? How does the Mu'tazilite error of demanding grace from God echo in your own expectations? What would it take for you to surrender that sense of entitlement entirely?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they could dispute with God if He didn't give them what they deemed best. In what areas of your life do you find yourself silently 'arguing' with God's decree? Will you sit with that resistance right now and consciously submit to His will?
?
The Prophet called those who obligate God 'adversaries of God.' Does your heart ever hold God to account for unanswered prayers or perceived injustices? How can you transform that adversarial stance into one of complete trust in His wisdom, even when you don't understand?

The Mu'tazila Doctrine of al-Lutf
Sanusi defines the Mu'tazila concept of lutf and al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definition of al-lutf and their principle of al-aslah (most beneficial).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila held that God must create grace to incline people to obedience without compulsion.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the key Mu'tazila doctrine of al-lutf (grace) and al-aslah (most beneficial). Understanding these terms is essential for grasping the core difference between Ash'ari and Mu'tazila theology. Let's read the definition carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic and translation of the definition of al-lutf.
Notice the terms used: لطف, أصلح, إلجاء.
2
Identify the two key obligations
The Mu'tazila made two things obligatory on God: observing al-aslah and creating al-lutf.
3
Understand the limit of lutf
Al-lutf inclines towards obedience but does not compel; it stops short of ilja'.
This preserves human free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدُّ اللطفِ الواجب الذي قالَتْ بهِ المعتزلة ]
وخالفهمُ المعتزلة ؛ فأوجبوا مراعاة الأصلح للعباد ، وأوجبوا اللطف ؛ وهو خلْقُ الشيء الذي يُوجِبُ للمُكلَّف ترجيح جانبِ الطاعةِ مِنْ غيرِ أنْ ينتهي إلى حدّ الإلجاء ، وأوجبوا إكمال عقل مَنْ أَرادَ تكليفه ، وإقدارَهُ ، وإزاحة العلل عنه التي تمنعُهُ مِنْ أَداءِ مَا كُلَّفَ بِهِ ، حتى إنَّهُ لو أَخَلَّ بذلك لكانَتْ لهم خصومة له ومطالبة بحقهم ! تعالى الله عمَّا يقولُ الظالمون علوّاً كبيراً .
ولقد صدق فيهم قوله صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : " الْقَدَرِيَّةُ خُصَمَاءُ اللهِ فِي الْقَدَرِ »(۱)
The definition of the obligatory grace (al-lutf) which the Mu'tazila held: They - the Mu'tazila - opposed [the Ash'aris]; so they made it obligatory to observe what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for the servants, and they made obligatory the grace, which is the creation of something that necessitates for the responsible person the preference of the side of obedience without reaching the limit of compulsion, and they made it obligatory to perfect the intellect of whomever He intends to charge, and to enable him, and to remove the impediments that prevent him from performing what he is charged with, so much so that if He failed to do that, they would have a dispute with Him and a demand of their right! Exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, with a great exaltation. And indeed the saying of him, peace and blessings be upon him, is true concerning them: 'The Qadiriyya are the adversaries of God concerning predestination.'
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazila concept of al-lutf (grace) and enumerates their obligations on God. The author first states that the Mu'tazila disagreed with the Ash'aris by making it obligatory for God to observe what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. Then he defines al-lutf as the creation of something that inclines the morally responsible person (mukallaf) towards obedience without compulsion (ilja'). They also hold that God must perfect the intellect of those He intends to charge, grant them power, and remove impediments. The author then condemns this view, saying that if God failed in these, the Mu'tazila would have a dispute with Him—a notion he deems exalting God above. Finally, he cites a hadith calling the Qadiriyya (a term for Mu'tazila) the 'adversaries of God concerning predestination,' affirming the truth of this description.

Key Terms
لطف
أصلح
إلجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what distinguishes al-lutf from compulsion?
A) It is the creation of obedience itself.
B) It does not reach the limit of ilja'.
C) It removes all obstacles.
D) It is purely intellectual.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila assert that God must provide grace that guides without forcing, as part of His obligation to do what is most beneficial for His creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you felt a strong inner pull to do something good, even though it was difficult. Did you recognize that moment as a gift from God (lutf), or did you assume it was entirely your own resolve? How might your relationship with God change if you saw every good inclination as His direct assistance? Starting today, before each act of worship, pause and silently acknowledge, 'If You do not incline me, I cannot even begin.'
?
If you truly believed that God is obligated to give you what is most beneficial in every situation, would you still complain about hardships? Where in your life are you questioning God's plan for you? Take a specific struggle and ask yourself: 'Is it possible that this very difficulty is the most beneficial thing for me?' Commit today to trust His choice over your own.
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that God must provide grace to everyone. Do you live as though God is constantly guiding you, or do you assume He helps only occasionally? When you last sinned, did you consider that God may have withheld His lutf as a test? How would your repentance be different if you actively begged Him to send you His grace and not leave you alone?

The Mu'tazila Obligations on God and the Ash'ari Rebuttal
Sanusi lists Mu'tazila obligations on God and cites a hadith condemning them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional obligations the Mu'tazila impose on God (perfection of intellect, enabling, removal of impediments) and the Ash'ari criticism including the hadith.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila go further: God must perfect intellect, grant power, and remove obstacles—otherwise they would dispute His justice.

Sanusi continues enumerating the Mu'tazila obligations on God beyond al-lutf. These include perfecting the intellect, granting ability, and removing barriers. He then condemns this view as tantamount to disputing with God, citing a prophetic hadith that labels the Qadiriyya (the Mu'tazila) as adversaries of God concerning predestination.

Guided Path
1
List the three obligations

These are in addition to al-lutf.
2
Understand the consequence
If God failed to do these, the Mu'tazila would have a dispute with Him.
3
Read the condemnation
Sanusi exalts God above such claims.
Note the phrase 'Exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say.'
4
Reflect on the hadith
The Prophet called the Qadiriyya 'adversaries of God concerning predestination.'
This is a strong condemnation of the Mu'tazila position.

Sanusi Commends the Ash'ari-Jubba'i Debate
Sanusi's commendatory remark about a historical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a brief commendation of a historical debate to reinforce the Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
A single exclamation can highlight a whole debate's persuasive power.

In this very short passage, Sanusi inserts a glowing remark about a famous debate between Imam al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazili leader al-Jubba'i. He does not provide the debate's content but calls it 'excellent' – an exclamation that serves as a rhetorical seal on the preceding rational arguments. This lesson examines the function of such a reference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the tone of admiration: 'wa ma ahsana...' (how excellent).
2
Identify what is and isn't given
The passage only supplies the participants and a vague reference to 'the issue'. The debate content is not included; the author assumes the reader knows it or will find it elsewhere.
The footnote markers indicate the debate may continue outside this text.
3
Understand the rhetorical purpose
Sanusi uses this exclamation to conclude a rational discussion with a concrete historical example, implying that the Ash'ari position was convincingly demonstrated.
It is a device to link abstract reasoning to real argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أحسنَ مناظرةً وقعَتْ بين الشيخ الأشعري والجبائي في مسألة (۱) (۲) ٥٢٤
And how excellent is a debate that occurred between Shaykh al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i concerning the issue.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage is a brief introductory remark about a famous debate between Imam al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila leader al-Jubba'i on the topic of divine obligation (al-aslah). The author commends the debate as an excellent example. However, the content of the debate is not included in this text; it is only referenced. The footnote markers and page number indicate that the debate likely continues or is cited elsewhere. This serves as a rhetorical conclusion to the rational arguments, pointing to a historical demonstration of the Ash'ari position.

Key Terms
مناظرة
الأشعري
الجبائي

Key Takeaway
Sanusi uses a brief commendation of the debate between al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i to highlight the persuasiveness of the Ash'ari argument, without detailing the debate itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi praises this debate as 'excellent' without giving you any of its content. ?

If the former, what changed in your heart; if the latter, what might you be missing?
?
Sanusi's exclamation suggests that the power of the Ash'ari argument was so clear that it deserved praise. Think of a time when you were convinced of a truth not by a detailed proof but by a powerful display of reasoning. Has that conviction translated into action and worship, or has it remained an intellectual assent? How can you ensure that your theological convictions transform your daily life?

The Anecdote of Al-Jubba'i and the Two Boys
Sanusi narrates the anecdote of Al-Jubba'i and the boy's question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili objection to divine predestination based on the case of a child dying before the age of obligation.

Core Idea
Mu'tazili rationalism challenges God's justice by questioning why some die young.

The Ash'ari theologian Al-Jubba'i is confronted with a narrative that exposes the limits of human reason when judging divine actions. A boy who died before religious maturity asks why God caused him to die early, knowing he would disbelieve later. This story sets up the Mu'tazili dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the encounter between Al-Jubba'i and the two boys. Let the narrative unfold before analyzing it.
2
Understand the boy's question
Identify the theological problem: the boy died before taklif (religious obligation) yet was punished, and God knew he would disbelieve. Why was he not given a chance?
This is a classic Mu'tazili objection based on human notions of justice.
3
Recognize Al-Jubba'i's silence
Notice that Al-Jubba'i was confounded (فبُهِتَ) and could not answer. This silence indicates the rational impasse that Mu'tazili reasoning leads to.
The story is meant to show the failure of relying solely on human reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما لنا هذا الصبي نالم تمثنا صغاراً قبل التكليف وقد علمتَ منَّا الكفرَ بعده كما فعلت بهذا الصبي ؟! فبُهِتَ الجبائي ، ولم يَقدِرْ أنْ يجيب بكلمةٍ فقالَ لهُ الشيخُ رضيَ اللهُ عنهُ : وقفَ حمارُ الشيخ في العقبة ثم قالَ : تعالى أنْ تُوزَنَ أحكامُ ذي الجلال بميزان الاعتزال وأمَّا المنقولُ : فقولُهُ تعالى : ﴿ لَا يُسْأَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْأَلُونَ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٣] ، وكقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿وَلَوْ شَاءَ رَبُّكَ لَجَعَلَ النَّاسَ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً﴾ [هود : ١١٨] ، ونحو ذلك ممَّا هو كثير .
Why do we have this boy? He died small before the age of obligation, and you knew from us disbelief after it as you did with this boy?! So Al-Jubba'i was confounded and could not answer a word. The Shaykh, may Allah be pleased with him, said to him: "The donkey of the Shaykh stopped at the slope." Then he said: "Exalted is He above weighing the judgments of the Majestic One by the scale of Mu'tazilism. As for transmitted proof: His statement, Exalted: 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned' [Al-Anbiya 23]; and His statement, Exalted: 'And if your Lord had willed, He would have made mankind one community' [Hud 118]; and similar verses which are many."
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage is a narrative refutation of Mu'tazili rationalism. It begins with a story: a young boy who died before the age of religious obligation (taklif) questions God: "Why did You cause me to die small before taklif, and You knew that I would disbelieve after that, as You did with this other boy?" This is a classic Mu'tazili objection about divine predestination and justice. The boy implies that God unjustly prevented him from a longer life in which he could have disbelieved and been punished. The speaker here is apparently the Mu'tazili theologian Al-Jubba'i, who is posed this question and is left speechless ("فبُهِتَ الجبائي"). The Ash'ari Shaykh then responds with a witty remark: "The donkey of the Shaykh stopped at the slope" — meaning that the Mu'tazili's rational argument has come to a halt like a donkey stuck on a hill. He then declares that God's judgments are too exalted to be weighed by the scale of Mu'tazilism, which subjects divine actions to human rational standards. He then cites two Quranic verses as transmitted proofs: "He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned" (21:23) affirming that God's actions are beyond human interrogation; and "If your Lord had willed, He would have made mankind one community" (11:118) showing that divine will is supreme. The lesson concludes that such evidence is abundant.

Key Terms
الجبائي
الاعتزال
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story suggests that God is unjust for not giving the boy a chance to disbelieve and be punished.
Correction: The story is meant to expose the limits of human reason; it does not affirm that God is unjust but rather that His justice is beyond human standards.

Checkpoint
Why was Al-Jubba'i unable to answer the boy's question?
A) He lost his voice
B) The argument exposed a flaw in his rationalist framework
C) He was not a theologian
D) The boy was lying

Key Takeaway
The anecdote shows that Mu'tazili rationalism fails when confronted with the mystery of divine decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unfair or makes you question God's wisdom, do you react like the boy in the story—demanding an explanation—or like Al-Jubba'i—falling silent? What would it take for you to trust that God's decree is just even when you cannot understand it?
?
Have you ever relied on your own reasoning to solve a spiritual dilemma, only to find that your intellect fell short? Where in your life right now are you trying to rationalize your way out of submitting to Allah's plan?
?
If you were to meet someone who is angry at God because of a tragedy, how would this anecdote equip you to respond? Would you offer a rational argument or point to the limits of human understanding? Can you honestly say you live by that conviction?

The Ash'ari Refutation: God's Actions Beyond Questioning
Sanusi presents the Ash'ari refutation and Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response that God's actions are not subject to human rational standards, as shown by the donkey metaphor and Quranic proofs.

Core Idea
God's judgments are too exalted for the scale of human reasoning.

After Al-Jubba'i is confounded, the Shaykh delivers a sharp retort and scriptural evidence. The donkey metaphor illustrates the failure of Mu'tazili rationalism, and the Quranic verses confirm that God is not questioned about His actions.

Guided Path
1
Study the donkey metaphor
Read the Shaykh's statement 'وقف حمار الشيخ في العقبة' and understand its rhetorical meaning: the Mu'tazili argument has come to a halt like a donkey stuck on a slope.
This is a clever dismissal of the rationalist approach.
2
Learn the principle of divine transcendence
The Shaykh declares that God is exalted above having His judgments weighed by the scale of Mu'tazilism. This is the core Ash'ari stance: God's actions are not subject to human scrutiny.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
Two verses are cited: 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned' (21:23) and 'If your Lord had willed, He would have made mankind one community' (11:118). See how they support the Ash'ari position.
These verses are clear textual evidence for the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage is a narrative refutation of Mu'tazili rationalism. It begins with a story: a young boy who died before the age of religious obligation (taklif) questions God: "Why did You cause me to die small before taklif, and You knew that I would disbelieve after that, as You did with this other boy?" The speaker here is apparently the Mu'tazili theologian Al-Jubba'i, who is posed this question and is left speechless ("فبُهِتَ الجبائي"). The Ash'ari Shaykh then responds with a witty remark: "The donkey of the Shaykh stopped at the slope" — meaning that the Mu'tazili's rational argument has come to a halt like a donkey stuck on a hill. The lesson concludes that such evidence is abundant.

Key Terms
لا يُسأل عما يفعل
الاعتزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The donkey metaphor is merely a joke without theological substance.
Correction: The metaphor is a rhetorical device that highlights the rational impasse of Mu'tazilism and sets up the serious doctrinal response.

Checkpoint
What is the function of the donkey metaphor in the refutation?
A) It suggests the Shaykh's donkey was stubborn
B) It symbolizes the halt of the Mu'tazili rational argument
C) It is a random comment with no meaning
D) It praises Al-Jubba'i's intelligence

Key Takeaway
God's actions are beyond human rational standards, as affirmed by Quranic proofs and the Ash'ari principle of divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a trial strikes you and you do not understand why, do you find yourself measuring God's action by your own scale, demanding an explanation? How can you train your heart to truly rest in the verse 'He is not questioned about what He does' and silence the inner Mu'tazili?
?
Is there an area of your faith where you rely more on your own reasoning than on what Allah has revealed—perhaps in matters of decree or divine wisdom? What concrete step will you take this week to surrender that area to God's unassailable authority?
?
Sanusi's metaphor of the donkey stopping at the slope shows the limit of human reasoning. In what specific situation are you most tempted to 'halt' and demand that God's actions make sense to you before you submit? Will you say today, 'I do not need to understand; I only need to trust'? Write a personal resolution.

Linguistic Origins and Theological Status of Nubuwwa
Sanusi explains the linguistic origins and theological definition of nubuwwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two linguistic derivations of 'nubuwwa' and the Ash'ari position that prophethood is a divine gift, neither inherent nor acquired.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a divine gift, not a natural trait nor something earned by effort.

Sanusi begins his discussion of prophethood by examining the word itself. He shows that the Arabic term carries meanings of both information and elevation, then clarifies a crucial theological boundary: prophethood is not part of a prophet's essence (as some claim) nor something one can attain through personal purification. This lesson unpacks that foundational point.

Guided Path
1
Examine the two linguistic roots
Read the Arabic passage and notice how Sanusi distinguishes between the hamz and non-hamz derivations of 'nubuwwa'. Pay attention to the different meanings: news and elevation.
The linguistic analysis sets the stage for the theological discussion.
2
Understand why prophethood is not essential
Sanusi refutes the Karramiyya claim that prophethood is an essential attribute of the prophet. Consider what 'essential' means: a property that cannot be separated from the person.
3
See why prophethood is not acquired
Sanusi refutes the philosophical view that prophethood can be attained through purification. He emphasizes that it is purely a matter of divine selection.
This counters the idea that spiritual effort alone can make one a prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الأولى في معنى النبوَّة والنبي لفظ النبوَّة في اللغة على وجهين : مهموز ، وغير مهموز : فأما في لغة مَنْ همَزَ : فهو مأخوذٌ مِنَ النبأ ؛ وهو الخبرُ ، ويحتمل أنْ يكونَ فعيل بمعنى مفعول أي : مُنْبَأُ بالغيوبِ ، أو هو بمعنى فاعل أو مُفْعِل أي : هو مُنْبِيءٌ بما أطلعه الله تعالى عليه ، ويصح تركُ الهمزة في هذين الوجهين تسهيلاً وأما في لغة مَنْ لم يهمزهُ مِنْ أصل : فهو مأخوذُ مِنَ النَّبْوَةِ بفتح النون ؛ وهو ما ارتفعَ مِنَ الأرضِ ، يُقالُ : نبا الشيء ؛ إذا ارتفعَ . فالمعنى على هذا : أنَّ النبيَّ مُرتفع عن طورِ البشر باختصاصه بالوحي وخطاب الله تعالى وليست النبوة صفةً ذاتيةً للنبي كما صارَ إليه الكرَّاميَّة ، ولا مكتسبةً كما صارَ إِليهِ الفلاسفةُ ؛ فإنَّهم يرون التزكية والتحلية صِقَالاً في مرآة النفس إلى أن يتهيَّأ لما لا يتهيَّأُ لإدراكه غيرُه ، وإِنَّما مرجع النبوَّةِ إلى اصطفاء الله عبداً مِنْ عبادِهِ بالوحي إليه .
The first issue: concerning the meaning of prophethood and prophet. The word 'prophethood' in the language has two aspects: with hamz and without hamz. As for the language of those who use hamz: it is derived from 'naba'' which is news, and it is possible that it is of the pattern fa'il in the sense of maf'ul, i.e., 'informed of unseen things', or it is in the sense of fa'il or muf'il, i.e., 'one who informs of what Allah the Exalted has disclosed to him', and it is permissible to drop the hamz in these two aspects for ease. As for the language of those who do not use hamz from the root: it is derived from 'nabwa' (with fatha on the nun), which is that which rises from the ground; it is said 'naba al-shay' if it rises. So the meaning according to this is that the prophet is elevated above the level of humans by his specialization with revelation and the speech of Allah the Exalted. And prophethood is not an essential attribute of the prophet, as the Karramiyya held, nor is it acquired, as the philosophers held; for they consider purification and adornment as polishing the mirror of the soul until it becomes capable of what others cannot perceive. Rather, the reference of prophethood is to Allah's selection of a servant from His servants by revelation to him.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage is the first of the three issues Sanusi enumerated: the definition of nubuwwa and nabi. He begins with a linguistic analysis. The word 'nubuwwa' has two origins: with hamz (from naba', meaning news) or without hamz (from nabwa, meaning elevation). For the first origin, the word could be understood as either 'one who is informed' (maf'ul) or 'one who informs' (fa'il or muf'il). For the second origin, the prophet is one elevated above ordinary human status due to receiving revelation. After the linguistic definitions, Sanusi turns to a theological clarification: nubuwwa is not an essential attribute (dhatiyya) inherent in the prophet, as the Karramiyya sect claimed; nor is it something acquired (muktasaba) through self-purification, as the philosophers held. The philosophers viewed purification as polishing the soul's mirror to attain special knowledge. Instead, Sanusi states that nubuwwa is solely a matter of divine selection (istifa') by which God chooses a servant and communicates revelation to him. This refutes both extremes and establishes the Ash'ari position that prophethood is a gift from God, not a natural property or earned status.

Key Terms
النبوة
النبي
الكرامية
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood can be attained through spiritual purification and moral effort.
Correction: Sanusi states that prophethood is not acquired (مكتسبة); it is solely by Allah's selection (اصطفاء) and revelation, not through any human effort.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, from which root is 'nabi' derived for those who do not use hamz?
A) From naba' meaning news.
B) From nabwa meaning elevation.
C) From nubuwwa meaning choice.
D) From risala meaning message.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is a divine gift from Allah, not an inherent trait or an acquired skill.

The Primary Distinction Between Miracles and Charismatic Wonders
Sanusi states the primary distinction between mu'jiza and karama: accompaniment of prophetic claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary distinction between a miracle (mu'jiza) and a charismatic wonder (karama) based on the accompaniment of a prophetic claim.

Core Idea
A miracle is defined by its connection to a claim of prophethood; without that claim, it's not a miracle.

This passage from Sanusi's work establishes a clear criterion for distinguishing miracles from other supernormal events. He explains that the key factor is whether the event accompanies a claim to prophethood. Understanding this distinction is essential for proper Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'mu'jiza', 'karama', 'da'wa al-risala'. Understand their meanings as they are used in this passage.
3
Understand the distinction
Sanusi states that the miracle is defined as 'accompanying the claim of the message'. Contrast this with events that occur without a claim or with a claim to sainthood—these are karama, not mu'jiza.
The key is that karama does not accompany a claim to prophethood.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why is this distinction important? How does it remove confusion between miracles and charismatic wonders?
This distinction protects the unique status of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبقولِهِ : ( مُقارِنٌ لدعوى الرسالة ) مما وقع بدونِ دعوى ، أو بدعوى غير دعوى الرسالة ؛ كدعوى الولاية هذا الذي ذكرتُ ممَّا تتميّزُ بهِ المعجزة عن الكرامة ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ الكرامة وإنْ كانَتْ أمراً خارقاً للعادة فإنَّها لا تكون مقارنةً لدعوى النبوة ، وبهذا يزولُ اللَّبْسُ بينهما
And by his saying: 'accompanying the claim of the message' – what occurs without a claim, or with a claim other than the claim of the message, such as the claim of sainthood. This is what I have mentioned by which the miracle is distinguished from the charismatic wonder. That is because the charismatic wonder, even if it is a supernormal event, does not accompany the claim of prophethood. And by this the confusion between them is removed.
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This passage defines the primary distinction between a miracle (mu'jiza) and a charismatic wonder (karama). It begins by quoting a definition: a miracle is 'accompanying the claim of the message' – meaning it occurs as proof of a claim to prophethood. If a supernormal event occurs without any claim, or with a claim other than prophethood (like a claim to sainthood), it is not a miracle. The author confirms this as the key distinguishing feature: the karama, though supernormal, does not accompany a claim to prophethood, thus removing confusion between the two.

Key Terms
معجزة
كرامة
دعوى الرسالة
دعوى النبوة
خارق للعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that any supernormal event is a miracle.
Correction: This passage clarifies that only supernormal events that accompany a claim to prophethood are miracles; otherwise, they are charismatic wonders or other phenomena.

Checkpoint
What is the primary distinction between a mu'jiza and a karama according to this passage?
A) A mu'jiza is more powerful than a karama.
B) A mu'jiza accompanies a claim to prophethood, while a karama does not.
C) A mu'jiza is performed by prophets only, while a karama is performed by saints.
D) A mu'jiza is always visible, while a karama is hidden.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is uniquely defined by accompanying a claim to prophethood, which distinguishes it from a charismatic wonder.
